# Full solutions

## Case 001
> To prevent water erosion of river banks, they can be strengthened with stones. However, water gradually washes sand and soil from under the stones, and the shoreline collapses. What can we do?

### anthropic/claude-sonnet-4.5 — `P_L_TRIZ_OFF`
# DESIGN PROCESS

## 1. IDENTIFY THE CHALLENGE
The core trade-off: stones must be **heavy and immobile** (to resist water flow and protect the bank) yet must also be **permeable or allow substrate retention** (to prevent sand/soil washout from beneath). The stones successfully block surface erosion but create undermining by concentrating flow beneath them, causing collapse.

## 2. SEARCH FOR RESOURCES
Available resources:
- The stones themselves (mass, voids between them)
- Water flow (can be redirected or filtered)
- Natural materials on-site (smaller stones, gravel)
- Gravity (for settling and filtration)
- The layered structure of the bank protection system

## 3. APPLY DESIGN TACTICS
Primary tactic: **Nested systems / Segmentation with filtration gradient**
Create a multi-layer system where each layer has progressively finer material, allowing water to pass while trapping progressively smaller particles.

Secondary tactic: **Use of porous/permeable materials**
Replace impermeable barrier thinking with a filtering approach.

## 4. RESOLVE, DON'T TRADE OFF
Install a **geotextile filter fabric** beneath the stones. This fabric is permeable to water but blocks soil particles from migrating through. The system works as follows:
- Large stones on top resist water force
- Geotextile fabric underneath allows water drainage (preventing pressure buildup) while retaining soil
- The fabric's pores are sized to pass water molecules but trap soil particles
- Water flows through without carrying substrate away

Alternative enhancement: Add a graded stone layer (small gravel between fabric and large stones) for additional mechanical protection and load distribution.

## 5. MAXIMIZE VALUE
The geotextile solution adds minimal cost and complexity while completely resolving the undermining problem. It requires no maintenance, uses existing stone placement methods, and the fabric is a single thin layer that doesn't significantly increase installation effort. This delivers maximum protection with minimal added system.

---

FINAL SOLUTION:

Place a layer of geotextile filter fabric directly on the riverbank soil before positioning the protective stones on top. This fabric is a permeable textile material specifically engineered with tiny pores that allow water to flow through freely while blocking soil and sand particles from washing away. 

The installation process is straightforward: unroll the fabric along the bank, overlap adjacent sheets by about 12 inches, and immediately cover with the stone riprap. The fabric conforms to the bank contour and stays in place under the stone weight.

For enhanced durability in high-flow areas, add a 4-6 inch layer of small gravel between the fabric and the large stones. This graded layer distributes the stone weight more evenly and provides additional filtration.

The fabric remains permeable, so water pressure doesn't build up behind it, which would cause bulging or failure. Instead, water drains through while the soil structure stays intact beneath the stones.

**Why it works:** The geotextile fabric acts as a selective barrier—its pore size allows water molecules to pass through while physically trapping soil particles that are too large to fit through the openings. This prevents undermining while maintaining drainage, so the stones remain supported on stable ground indefinitely.

### anthropic/claude-sonnet-4.5 — `P_L_TRIZ_ON`
# TRIZ Analysis

## 1. IDENTIFY THE CONTRADICTION

**Technical contradiction:** We need to improve the **stability/strength** of the riverbank protection (preventing erosion), but this worsens when we try to prevent the **loss of material** (sand and soil) from beneath the stones. The stones protect the surface but create conditions where undermining occurs.

**Physical contradiction:** The protective layer must be **permeable** (to allow water flow and prevent pressure buildup) AND **impermeable** (to prevent sand/soil washout).

## 2. SEARCH FOR RESOURCES

Available resources:
- The stones themselves
- The flowing water and its energy
- The sand and soil being washed away
- Gravity
- The space between stones
- Natural materials in the environment (vegetation, smaller particles)

## 3. APPLY INVENTIVE PRINCIPLES

**Principle 3 - Local Quality:** Make different parts of the structure serve different functions. Create layers with different properties.

**Principle 7 - Nested Doll:** Place one protective system inside another.

**Principle 40 - Composite Materials:** Use a filtering layer that allows water through but blocks soil particles.

## 4. RESOLVE, DON'T TRADE OFF

Solution: Place a **geotextile filter fabric** beneath the stones. This permeable synthetic fabric allows water to pass through freely (maintaining drainage and preventing pressure buildup) while blocking soil particles from washing away. The fabric acts as a selective barrier - it separates by particle size, letting water molecules through while retaining sand and soil.

## 5. MAXIMIZE IDEALITY

This solution adds minimal cost and complexity (one thin layer), uses existing stone placement methods, requires no maintenance, and directly resolves the contradiction by making the barrier selectively permeable based on particle size rather than trading off between complete permeability and impermeability.

---

# FINAL SOLUTION:

Install a layer of geotextile filter fabric between the riverbank soil and the protective stones. This engineered fabric, made of permeable synthetic fibers, acts as a selective barrier that allows water to flow through freely while preventing sand and soil particles from washing away.

To implement: First, grade and prepare the riverbank slope. Roll out the geotextile fabric directly onto the soil surface, overlapping adjacent sheets by at least 12 inches. Secure the fabric at the top of the bank and anchor it periodically. Then place the stones on top of the fabric as usual, ensuring good coverage.

The fabric's porous structure lets water pass through in both directions, preventing pressure buildup behind the stones that could destabilize the bank. However, the tiny openings in the fabric are sized to trap soil and sand particles, holding them in place. This maintains the soil foundation beneath the stones while still allowing necessary drainage. The stones provide weight and abrasion protection for the fabric, while the fabric prevents the undermining that causes stone revetments to fail.

**Why it works:** The fabric creates a size-selective filter that separates water molecules from soil particles, allowing beneficial drainage while preventing harmful erosion, thus eliminating the underlying cause of collapse without blocking water flow.

### anthropic/claude-sonnet-4.5 — `P_S_TRIZ_OFF`
# Analysis and Solution Development

The problem involves a contradiction: stones protect the riverbank from surface erosion, but water undermines them by washing away the substrate, causing eventual collapse.

The key insight is to use a geotextile fabric layer beneath the stones. This permeable synthetic material allows water to pass through while preventing soil migration.

# FINAL SOLUTION:

Place a layer of geotextile fabric directly on the riverbank soil before adding the protective stones. This fabric is a permeable synthetic material with tiny pores that allow water to flow through freely while blocking soil particles from washing away. The fabric should extend across the entire bank area to be protected, with overlapping seams of at least 12 inches. Anchor the fabric edges securely into the bank. Then place riprap stones (typically 6-12 inches in diameter) on top of the fabric in a layer at least 18 inches thick. The stones provide weight and erosion resistance, while the fabric underneath acts as a filter barrier.

This solution works because the geotextile fabric creates a physical barrier that separates two different-sized materials. Water molecules and fine sediments can pass through the fabric's pores in one direction (drainage), but the soil particles beneath are too large to migrate upward and outward through the fabric. This prevents undermining while maintaining proper drainage, keeping both the stones and bank stable over time.

### anthropic/claude-sonnet-4.5 — `P_S_TRIZ_ON`
# TRIZ Analysis

The contradiction here is that stones protect the bank surface but cannot prevent undermining from below. This is a typical harmful effect that occurs over time.

Relevant TRIZ principles:
- **Principle 1 (Segmentation)**: Divide the protective system into parts
- **Principle 24 (Intermediary)**: Use an intermediate substance
- **Principle 35 (Parameter Changes)**: Change the physical state or properties

The key insight is to use a permeable fabric (geotextile) as an intermediary layer between the stones and soil. This creates a filtering barrier that allows water to pass through while retaining soil particles.

# Solution Approach

Place a geotextile fabric layer on the riverbank before adding stones. The fabric acts as a filter membrane - water can flow through its pores, but sand and soil particles are too large to pass. This prevents undermining while maintaining drainage. The stones on top hold the fabric in place and provide erosion resistance at the surface.

---

FINAL SOLUTION:

Install a layer of geotextile fabric directly on the riverbank soil before placing the protective stones on top. Geotextile is a permeable synthetic fabric specifically designed for soil stabilization. 

First, prepare the bank surface by removing loose debris and smoothing it. Then unroll the geotextile fabric to cover the entire area that needs protection, overlapping adjacent sheets by at least 12 inches. Secure the fabric with stakes or pins to prevent shifting. Finally, place the stones on top of the fabric layer as you normally would.

The fabric acts as a filter barrier with tiny pores. Water can flow freely through these pores, preventing pressure buildup behind the bank, but sand and soil particles are too large to pass through. This keeps the soil in place beneath the stones while allowing natural drainage. The stones above provide weight to hold everything in position and protect against surface erosion from flowing water and waves.

This creates a stable, long-lasting riverbank protection system that addresses both surface erosion and subsurface undermining.

### deepseek/deepseek-chat-v3.1 — `P_L_TRIZ_OFF`
1. IDENTIFY THE CHALLENGE: The trade-off is between structural stability (achieved by placing heavy stones to armor the bank) and erosion control (as water flows under the stones, washing away the underlying soil and eventually causing collapse). The stones provide protection but create a new failure mode by allowing under-scour.

2. SEARCH FOR RESOURCES: Available resources include the water flow itself (its kinetic energy and sediment load), the soil being eroded, and the stones already placed. The system also has gravity and the geometry of the bank.

3. APPLY DESIGN TACTICS: The most relevant tactic is "Prior Counteraction": introducing an element that counteracts the harmful effect (undermining) in advance. A secondary tactic is "Merging": combining the armor layer with a filtering function to address both surface and subsurface erosion simultaneously.

4. RESOLVE, DON'T TRADE OFF: Instead of just using larger stones (which are costly and still may be undermined), place a flexible, permeable geotextile fabric between the stones and the soil bank. This fabric acts as a filter, allowing water to seep through slowly but preventing the soil particles from being washed away, thus eliminating the undermining.

5. MAXIMIZE VALUE: This solution uses a simple, low-cost, and widely available material (geotextile) to augment the existing stone armor. It requires no new heavy machinery or complex systems, significantly extends the life of the revetment, and prevents failure with minimal added expense.

The solution works because the geotextile fabric performs a filtering function. Its porous structure reduces water pressure buildup by allowing drainage, but its tightly woven or non-woven fibers are sized to block the finer soil particles from passing through. This prevents the subsurface erosion that would otherwise hollow out the bank beneath the stones, while the stones themselves continue to protect the surface from the direct force of the water.

FINAL SOLUTION:
To protect the riverbank from collapsing, first prepare the slope by smoothing it to a stable angle. Then, lay down a strong, permeable fabric—called a geotextile—directly over the soil across the entire area to be protected. This fabric is designed to let water drain through while trapping the soil particles, preventing them from being washed away. On top of this fabric layer, place a thick covering of stones or rocks, building a sturdy armor layer. The weight of the stones holds the fabric firmly against the bank. Water can still flow through the gaps between the stones and through the fabric itself, relieving pressure, but the soil beneath is shielded. The fabric acts as a filter, stopping the sand and silt from being pulled out from under the rocks. This combination keeps the foundation solid, so the stone covering remains in place and the bank stays intact for a much longer time, even during strong currents and floods.

### deepseek/deepseek-chat-v3.1 — `P_L_TRIZ_ON`
**Step 1: Identify the Contradiction**  
The technical contradiction is between **stability** (improved by placing stones to armor the bank) and **stability** (worsened as water washes away underlying soil, causing collapse). This is a physical contradiction: the soil under the stones must be **present** to support the stones, but **absent** to allow water flow without erosion.

**Step 2: Search for Resources**  
Available resources include the stones themselves, the water flow, sand/silt in the water, vegetation, and gravity. The system already contains granular material (sand/soil) that is being removed; perhaps it can be trapped or replaced with something that resists washout.

**Step 3: Apply Inventive Principles**  
The contradiction maps to **Principle 24: Intermediary** (use an intermediate material or process) and **Principle 40: Composite Materials** (replace homogeneous material with composite). Also relevant is **Principle 31: Porous Materials** (make an object porous or add porous elements).

**Step 4: Resolve, Don’t Trade Off**  
Instead of trying to prevent all water flow under stones (which increases pressure and leads to failure), allow controlled flow while trapping soil. Use a porous, flexible intermediary between stones and soil—a geotextile fabric or a mesh—that lets water pass but retains fine particles. Over time, trapped silt reinforces the bank naturally.

**Step 5: Maximize Ideality**  
The solution adds minimal new material (geotextile), uses existing water flow to deposit and hold soil, and improves stability without blocking drainage. It turns the problem (silt in water) into part of the solution.

---

**FINAL SOLUTION:**

Line the riverbank soil with a permeable, durable geotextile fabric before placing the stone layer. This fabric acts like a filter: it allows water to seep through slowly, preventing pressure buildup behind the stones, but it catches fine sand and soil particles that would otherwise wash away. Over time, these trapped particles accumulate within and behind the fabric, naturally filling voids and creating a reinforced, stable matrix that locks the stones in place. The fabric also helps distribute the weight of the stones more evenly, reducing settling. For additional strength in high-flow areas, the geotextile can be anchored with biodegradable stakes or tucked into trenches at the top and toe of the bank. Vegetation can be planted through cuts in the fabric to add root reinforcement.

This works because the fabric solves the core issue of subsurface erosion without trying to completely block water. It filters and retains the soil while allowing drainage, so the stones stay supported and the bank gradually becomes more stable as natural silt builds up behind the barrier.

A durable, permeable geotextile cloth is then rolled out and anchored over the entire prepared bank surface. On top of this fabric, a layered stone structure is built. The base layer consists of smaller gravel, which is then covered by larger, heavy rocks (riprap) that interlock to form a durable, heavy armor layer.

This works because the geotextile fabric acts as a robust filter. It is permeable, allowing water to seep through naturally, which prevents dangerous pressure buildup behind the structure. However, its tightly woven fabric prevents the underlying soil particles from being washed out by the flowing water. The stones protect the fabric from mechanical damage and absorb the direct energy of the current, while the fabric ensures the foundational soil remains intact, creating a stable, composite system that resorts erosion from both water flow and subsurface seepage.

FINAL SOLUTION:
To prevent the shoreline from collapsing as water washes away soil beneath stones, a strong, permeable fabric can be placed under the rocks. First, the eroded riverbank is smoothed to a stable angle. A tough geotextile cloth is then laid down over the entire slope and secured at the top and bottom. This special fabric lets water pass through but blocks the tiny sand and soil particles, stopping them from being washed away. On top of this fabric, a foundation layer of smaller gravel is placed, followed by a main layer of large, interlocking rocks called riprap. These heavy stones absorb the force of the river's current and protect the fabric from damage. Together, the fabric and stones form a single, reinforced structure. The fabric keeps the foundation soil in place, while the rocks shield the bank from the direct impact of the water, preventing the undermining that causes collapse. This creates a long-lasting, natural-looking barrier that stabilizes the bank.

### deepseek/deepseek-chat-v3.1 — `P_S_TRIZ_ON`
One approach is to use the TRIZ principle of merging or combining functions. Instead of just placing stones, we can integrate them with a flexible, permeable, and root-like matrix. A concrete solution is to use articulated concrete blocks or geocellular confinement systems (like geowebs) filled with soil and vegetation. These systems are interconnected to form a continuous mat. They are placed on the bank and anchored into the stable ground behind. The open cells are then filled with a soil mix and planted with native, deep-rooting riparian vegetation.

This works because it addresses the core contradiction: we need stones for strength and erosion resistance, but their rigidity and gaps allow undermining. The merged system provides structural integrity from the interconnected blocks or geocells, preventing individual stone displacement. Simultaneously, the soil fill and plant roots create a living matrix that binds the entire structure together and to the underlying bank. The roots grow through the cells, reinforcing the soil beneath and around the structure, preventing washout. The permeability allows water drainage without pressure buildup, while vegetation absorbs wave energy. The system becomes more stable over time as the roots strengthen.

FINAL SOLUTION:
To protect the riverbank from collapsing as water washes away soil from under stones, use a system of interconnected, honeycomb-like compartments made from durable, flexible material. These compartments, often called geocells, are laid flat and anchored securely onto the prepared bank. Each open cell is then filled with a mixture of soil, sand, and gravel. Finally, plant native grasses, shrubs, or other deep-rooting water-loving plants directly into the filled cells.

The geocell structure acts like a large, flexible mat that holds the filler material firmly in place, preventing individual stones from being dislodged. More importantly, the plant roots grow extensively through the soil in the cells and down into the natural riverbank below. Over time, this network of living roots binds the entire protective layer—geocells, filler soil, and the original bank—into a single, reinforced mass. The roots physically trap soil particles, preventing them from being washed out from underneath. The plants also absorb the energy of flowing water, further reducing erosion. This creates a living wall that becomes naturally stronger as the vegetation establishes itself, providing a long-term, sustainable solution that works with the environment rather than against it.

### google/gemini-2.5-flash — `P_L_TRIZ_OFF`
1. **IDENTIFY THE CHALLENGE.**
The core technical trade-off is between **bank strengthening** and **bank stability**. Strengthening the bank with stones (improving bank strength) leads to the worsening of bank stability as water erodes the underlying soil (reducing stability). This is an internal design tension where the bank needs to be both strong (to resist erosion) and stable (to prevent collapse due to underlying soil loss).

2. **SEARCH FOR RESOURCES.**
*   **Existing substances:** Water, sand, soil, stones.
*   **Fields:** Gravity (pulling stones down), water flow (carrying away soil).
*   **Properties of materials:** Stones are heavy and resistant to erosion; sand/soil are fine-grained and easily transported by water.
*   **Empty space:** The voids between the stones.

3. **APPLY DESIGN TACTICS.**
The most relevant tactic here is **Segmentation** (dividing an object into independent parts) combined with **Intermediary** (introducing an intermediate element). The problem arises because the large, erosion-resistant stones are directly in contact with the fine, erodible soil. We need to prevent the fine soil from escaping through the gaps in the stones.

4. **RESOLVE, DON'T TRADE OFF.**
Instead of just placing stones directly on the soil, we can introduce an intermediate layer that allows water to pass through but retains the soil. This layer acts as a filter, segmenting the bank into distinct layers: the erodible soil, the filter layer, and the protective stone layer. This prevents the soil from being washed away while still allowing the stones to protect the bank from direct water forces.

5. **MAXIMIZE VALUE.**
The most valuable solution would use readily available, inexpensive materials and be easy to implement. Geotextile fabric or a graded filter layer of smaller aggregate are good candidates.

---

FINAL SOLUTION:
To effectively prevent river bank erosion and collapse, a permeable filter layer should be installed between the natural soil and the protective stones. First, the bank is prepared, and then a durable, water-permeable geotextile fabric is laid directly over the soil. This fabric acts as a barrier, allowing water to drain through but preventing fine sand and soil particles from washing away. Alternatively, a graded filter layer can be constructed using progressively larger aggregate materials, starting with fine gravel directly on the soil, followed by coarser gravel, and finally the larger stones on top. This multi-layered approach ensures that the underlying soil remains intact while the outer stone layer absorbs the direct force of the water, providing long-term stability and erosion control.

This solution works because it physically separates the erodible soil from the water flow while still allowing water pressure to dissipate. The geotextile or graded aggregate layer acts as a filter, holding the soil particles in place even as water moves through the bank, thereby preventing the undermining of the protective stone layer.

### google/gemini-2.5-flash — `P_L_TRIZ_ON`
Here's a TRIZ-based approach to solving the river bank erosion problem:

1.  **IDENTIFY THE CONTRADICTION.**
    *   **Characteristic Improves:** The *strength* and *durability* of the river bank (by adding stones).
    *   **Characteristic Worsens:** The *stability* and *integrity* of the underlying soil/sand (due to water washing it away).
    *   **Contradiction:** To increase the *strength* of the bank (with stones), the *stability* of the foundation (soil) is decreased. The stones are intended to protect, but their presence, without proper foundation, leads to collapse. This is a physical contradiction: the bank needs to be both permeable (to allow water to drain without building pressure) and impermeable (to prevent soil erosion).

2.  **SEARCH FOR RESOURCES.**
    *   **Existing substances:** Stones, sand, soil, water.
    *   **Fields:** Gravity (water flow), pressure (hydrostatic pressure).
    *   **Properties:** Porosity of sand/soil, weight of stones, flow dynamics of water.
    *   **By-products:** Eroded sand/soil.

3.  **APPLY INVENTIVE PRINCIPLES.**
    *   **Principle 2: Taking Out.** Separate the interfering part (eroding water flow from the soil) from the useful part (stones protecting the bank).
    *   **Principle 3: Local Quality.** Change the structure of the object (the bank) from uniform to non-uniform, adapting each part to conditions best suited for its operation.
    *   **Principle 7: "Nested Doll" (Matryoshka).** Place one object inside another.
    *   **Principle 14: Spheroidality – Curvature.** Replace linear parts with curved ones; move from flat surfaces to spherical ones; use rollers, balls, spirals. (This can help distribute pressure and reduce direct erosive forces).
    *   **Principle 22: "Blessing in Disguise" (Convert Harm into Benefit).** Use the very water that causes erosion to help stabilize the bank.
    *   **Principle 32: Changing the Color.** (Not directly applicable here, but a common principle).
    *   **Principle 35: Parameter Changes.** Change the physical state of the object.

    Let's focus on **Principle 2 (Taking Out)** and **Principle 7 (Nested Doll)**, combined with **Principle 3 (Local Quality)**. The core idea is to separate the stones from the fine soil while still allowing water to pass through.

4.  **RESOLVE, DON'T TRADE OFF.**
    The problem is that water *directly* interacts with the fine sand/soil *under* the stones. If we can create a barrier that allows water to pass but retains the soil, the contradiction is resolved. A permeable layer between the stones and the soil would achieve this.

5.  **MAXIMIZE IDEALITY.**
    The ideal solution would use existing materials or minimal new components. Geotextile fabric is a common, highly effective, and relatively inexpensive solution that fits these principles. It acts as a filter, allowing water to drain while preventing soil particles from migrating.

***

FINAL SOLUTION:
To effectively protect river banks, a robust, permeable fabric layer should be installed directly on the prepared soil surface before placing any stones. This specialized fabric acts as a filter, allowing water to drain freely from the bank and through the stone layer, preventing hydrostatic pressure buildup. Crucially, its fine mesh structure physically separates the underlying sand and soil from the flowing water, preventing fine particles from being washed away. The stones are then placed on top of this fabric, providing the necessary weight and armor against the river's current. This system ensures the soil remains stable and intact, maintaining the structural integrity of the bank while the stones absorb the direct force of the water.

### google/gemini-2.5-flash — `P_S_TRIZ_OFF`
A single, physically realizable solution involves the strategic placement of a geotextile fabric beneath the stone riprap. This fabric acts as a permeable barrier, allowing water to pass through while retaining the underlying soil and sand particles. The stones are then placed directly on top of this geotextile layer, providing the necessary weight and structural integrity to resist the erosive forces of the river. The fabric should extend sufficiently up the bank and be anchored at the top to prevent undermining.

This solution works because the geotextile separates the protective stone layer from the erodible soil. The fabric's permeability prevents hydrostatic pressure buildup, which could otherwise lift the stones, while its fine mesh prevents the migration of soil particles. This maintains the integrity of the bank's foundation, ensuring the stones remain in place and the shoreline is stabilized long-term.

FINAL SOLUTION:
To prevent riverbank erosion, first prepare the bank by clearing debris and grading it to a stable slope. Then, lay down a continuous sheet of durable, permeable fabric directly onto the prepared soil. This fabric should extend from below the water line up the bank to a point well above the anticipated high-water mark, and be securely anchored at the top edge. Overlap any seams in the fabric by at least a foot to ensure full coverage. Finally, carefully place large, angular stones directly on top of this fabric, creating a robust, interlocking layer. The stones should be heavy enough to resist displacement by water flow and cover the fabric completely. This creates a stable, long-lasting barrier that protects the underlying soil from being washed away.

### google/gemini-2.5-flash — `P_S_TRIZ_ON`
Here's a TRIZ-based solution to the problem:

The core contradiction is that the stones are needed to protect the bank (beneficial), but they also allow the erosion of underlying material (harmful). We need to prevent the sand/soil from escaping while still allowing water to pass through the stone layer.

Applying the TRIZ principle of "Segmentation" and "Local Quality," we can introduce a permeable barrier between the stones and the soil. This barrier should be strong enough to retain the soil particles but porous enough to allow water to drain, preventing hydrostatic pressure buildup. A geotextile fabric or a layer of fine gravel/coarse sand could serve this purpose. This creates a multi-layered system where each layer performs a specific function, preventing the harmful interaction between the stones and the fine soil.

This works by separating the functions. The stones provide the bulk and resistance to direct water flow, while the geotextile or fine aggregate layer acts as a filter, preventing the washout of the underlying soil. Water can still permeate, so pressure doesn't build up behind the stone layer, which could otherwise cause instability.

FINAL SOLUTION:
To protect riverbanks from erosion, we can use a layered approach. First, prepare the bank by smoothing it. Then, lay down a durable, permeable fabric, similar to a heavy-duty landscape fabric, directly onto the soil. This fabric should be strong enough to withstand the weight of stones and water flow, but porous enough to let water pass through. Over this fabric, carefully place the stones, ensuring they interlock well and cover the fabric completely. The fabric acts as a filter, holding the soil and sand in place while allowing water to drain through it and between the stones. This prevents the underlying material from being washed away, keeping the bank stable and preventing collapses, even during strong currents or floods.

The geotextile fabric is durable and can withstand the flow of water, ensuring long-term protection against erosion. By using this approach, the natural appearance of the riverbank is preserved, and the solution is both cost-effective and environmentally friendly.

## Case 002
> Any two-wheel scooter or bicycle remains stable only when in motion. In other words, these devices are "dynamically stable". However, when they stop, they fall down on their side. There are known systems that can stabilize spatial position using liquid equilibrium detectors and gyroscopic stabilizers. However, there is "nothing to stabilize" in a bicycle transversely – the only thing we can do is install a sophisticated "scales-like" system so as to make it possible, by constantly balancing, to keep the mass center of the system strictly within the support area. But that seems too complex and completely unjustified. Can we build a two-wheel vehicle which would be able to rapidly move along, carrying its "pilot" or "passenger" (whatever turns you on!), and keep upright without falling when forced to stop?

### anthropic/claude-sonnet-4.5 — `P_L_TRIZ_OFF`
# DESIGN PROCESS

## 1. IDENTIFY THE CHALLENGE
The core trade-off: A two-wheel vehicle needs a narrow wheelbase (two wheels in line) for dynamic mobility and maneuverability while moving, but this same narrow configuration provides insufficient support area for static stability when stopped—the vehicle tips over. The conflict is between dynamic performance (requiring two inline wheels) and static stability (requiring a wider support base).

## 2. SEARCH FOR RESOURCES
Available resources include:
- Gravity (always present, predictable)
- The ground surface (stable, always available)
- The transition time between moving and stopped states
- Empty space beside the vehicle
- The vehicle's own weight and structure
- Time dimension: the vehicle doesn't need stability features while moving

## 3. APPLY DESIGN TACTICS
Primary tactic: **Segmentation in time** - separate the stability requirements by operational state. The vehicle needs different support configurations when moving versus stopped.

Secondary tactic: **Taking out/adding** - deploy additional support only when needed, remove it when not needed.

Tertiary tactic: **Cheap short-living object** - use temporary, simple support elements that appear only during the stopped state.

## 4. RESOLVE, DON'T TRADE OFF
Solution: Install retractable outrigger wheels or stabilizing legs that automatically deploy when the vehicle decelerates below a threshold speed (e.g., 3-5 km/h) and retract when accelerating above that speed. 

The deployment mechanism uses the vehicle's own deceleration/acceleration as the trigger—either through:
- Centrifugal/inertial sensors that release spring-loaded outriggers when rotational speed drops
- Simple mechanical linkage to the wheel rotation that extends supports at low RPM
- Gravity-assisted drop-down wheels held up by airflow or centrifugal force while moving

This creates a temporary three or four-point support base only when stopped, without compromising the two-wheel configuration during motion.

## 5. MAXIMIZE VALUE
This solution adds minimal complexity—just spring-loaded or gravity-assisted deployable supports with simple speed-sensing triggers. No active balancing, no gyroscopes, no complex control systems. The supports are passive, reliable, and only engage when needed. The vehicle maintains full two-wheel performance while moving and gains automatic stability when stopped.

---

FINAL SOLUTION:

Install small retractable outrigger wheels or stabilizing feet on both sides of the two-wheel vehicle that automatically deploy when the vehicle slows down and retract when it speeds up. These supports can be spring-loaded and held in the retracted position by centrifugal force or airflow while the vehicle is moving at normal speeds. When the vehicle decelerates below approximately 3-5 kilometers per hour, the centrifugal force or airflow weakens, allowing the springs to push the outriggers down to the ground, creating a stable three or four-point stance. When the rider accelerates again, the increasing wheel rotation or forward motion generates enough force to lift and lock the outriggers back into their retracted position against the vehicle body. The mechanism requires no electronic controls or power source—it operates purely through mechanical response to the vehicle's motion state. The outriggers remain completely out of the way during normal riding, preserving maneuverability and the slim profile of a traditional two-wheel vehicle.

**Why it works:** This solution separates stability needs by operating state—the vehicle uses its efficient two-wheel configuration while moving but automatically gains a wider support base only when stopped, using the vehicle's own speed change as the trigger for transformation.

### anthropic/claude-sonnet-4.5 — `P_L_TRIZ_ON`
# TRIZ Analysis

## 1. IDENTIFY THE CONTRADICTION

**Technical contradiction:** We need the vehicle to have two wheels for dynamic efficiency during motion (improving speed, maneuverability, simplicity), but two wheels cannot provide static stability when stopped (worsening stability of the object). 

This maps to:
- **Improving parameter:** Speed, ease of operation, shape (streamlined two-wheel design)
- **Worsening parameter:** Stability of the object's position

There's also a **physical contradiction:** The support base must be narrow (two wheels for motion efficiency) AND wide (for static stability when stopped).

## 2. SEARCH FOR RESOURCES

Available resources:
- The stopping event itself (time resource)
- Ground contact points
- Gravity (field resource)
- The vehicle's existing structure and wheels
- The transition between motion and rest states
- Unused space near the wheels

## 3. APPLY INVENTIVE PRINCIPLES

**Principle 15 - Dynamics:** Make characteristics of an object or environment automatically adjust for optimal performance at each stage. The support configuration should change between motion and stationary states.

**Principle 1 - Segmentation:** Divide the support system into parts that deploy only when needed.

**Principle 10 - Prior Action:** Prepare the stabilizing action in advance, ready to deploy automatically.

**Principle 24 - Intermediary:** Use an intermediate element that can be easily engaged/disengaged.

## 4. RESOLVE, DON'T TRADE OFF

The solution: **Deploy auxiliary support wheels automatically when the vehicle stops.**

Install small retractable wheels or feet on both sides of the vehicle that automatically extend downward when speed drops below a threshold (detected by wheel rotation sensors or simple centrifugal mechanisms). These supports create a wide, stable base when stationary. When motion resumes and speed increases, the supports automatically retract upward (via spring mechanisms or centrifugal force), restoring the two-wheel configuration for efficient riding.

This resolves the contradiction completely: we get two-wheel efficiency during motion AND multi-point stability when stopped, without compromise.

## 5. MAXIMIZE IDEALITY

This solution has high ideality because:
- Uses minimal added components (small wheels/feet, simple deployment mechanism)
- Automatic operation requires no pilot action
- Exploits the existing transition between motion and rest as a control signal
- Adds stability function with minimal weight, cost, and complexity
- No sophisticated sensors or gyroscopes needed

---

FINAL SOLUTION:

Equip the two-wheel vehicle with small retractable stabilizer wheels mounted on both sides near the bottom of the frame. These auxiliary wheels remain tucked up and out of the way during normal riding. When the vehicle slows down and comes to a stop, the stabilizers automatically deploy downward, extending to touch the ground and create a stable four-point base that prevents tipping. The deployment can be triggered by a simple mechanical system that responds to wheel rotation speed—for example, spring-loaded arms held up by centrifugal force that drop down when rotation slows, or a basic sensor that releases a latch. When the rider accelerates again, the stabilizers automatically retract upward, either pulled by springs once centrifugal force returns or lifted by a simple linkage connected to the drivetrain. This gives you a vehicle that rides efficiently on two wheels while moving but stands securely on four contact points when stopped, combining the advantages of both configurations without requiring the rider to manually deploy kickstands or perform balancing acts.

**Why it works:** The solution separates the vehicle's configuration in time—two wheels for motion, four points for stability—automatically switching between states based on the natural transition between riding and stopping. This eliminates the contradiction by making the support system dynamic rather than fixed.

### anthropic/claude-sonnet-4.5 — `P_S_TRIZ_OFF`
Let me analyze this problem and develop a practical solution.

The challenge is to create a two-wheel vehicle that remains stable both when moving and when stationary, without requiring complex balancing systems.

**Solution Approach:**

The key insight is to change the support configuration when the vehicle stops. Instead of trying to maintain balance on two wheels, we can deploy additional support points that expand the base of support.

**Concrete Solution:**

Install retractable outrigger wheels or stabilizing legs on both sides of the vehicle. When the vehicle is in motion, these remain retracted flush against the body. When the vehicle slows below a certain speed (detected by a simple speed sensor), spring-loaded or motor-driven mechanisms automatically deploy these supports outward and downward. This creates a stable three or four-point contact with the ground. The outriggers can be small wheels (like training wheels) positioned near the center of mass, or simple feet that extend from the frame. When the rider accelerates again, the supports automatically retract. This system is already used successfully in some mobility scooters and can be implemented with simple mechanical linkages or basic actuators.

**Why it works:**
This solution transforms the vehicle from a dynamically stable two-point system into a statically stable multi-point system when stopped. By expanding the support base to include the center of mass within a polygon of support points, the vehicle cannot tip over, eliminating the need for active balancing.

FINAL SOLUTION:

Install retractable outrigger wheels or stabilizing legs on both sides of the two-wheel vehicle. When the vehicle moves at normal speeds, these supports remain folded up against the frame, keeping the vehicle streamlined and allowing normal two-wheel operation. As the vehicle slows down and approaches a stop, a simple speed sensor triggers the deployment mechanism. Spring-loaded arms or small motor-driven actuators automatically extend these supports outward and downward on both sides. The outriggers make contact with the ground before the vehicle comes to a complete stop, creating a stable three or four-point stance. These can be small wheels positioned near the vehicle's center of mass or simple padded feet. When the rider is ready to move again and accelerates, the supports automatically retract back into their stored position. This system requires no sophisticated electronics or active balancing—just basic mechanical components that respond to the vehicle's speed, similar to training wheels but deployed automatically only when needed.

This works because it changes how the vehicle achieves stability depending on its state. While moving, the vehicle relies on dynamic stability from forward motion. When stopped, the extended supports create a wide base that encompasses the center of mass, providing static stability through simple geometry rather than active balancing.

# TRIZ Analysis

The contradiction here is that a two-wheel vehicle needs to remain upright when stationary (requiring stability) but must maintain a narrow two-wheel configuration for dynamic motion. 

Using TRIZ Principle 17 (Another Dimension), we can move from 2D stability to 3D by adding deployable support wheels that extend when stopping.

Using TRIZ Principle 15 (Dynamics), we make the support system adaptive - retractable wheels that automatically deploy only when needed.

Using TRIZ Principle 1 (Segmentation), we separate the functions: two main wheels for motion, auxiliary wheels for stationary stability.

# Solution Approach

Install small retractable stabilizer wheels on both sides of the vehicle that automatically deploy when the vehicle slows below a threshold speed (e.g., 5 km/h). These wheels would be spring-loaded and controlled by a speed-sensing mechanism. When moving at normal speeds, they retract flush with the body. When stopping, they extend outward and downward to create a stable four-point contact with the ground.

---

FINAL SOLUTION:

Install small retractable stabilizer wheels mounted on both sides of the two-wheel vehicle's frame. These auxiliary wheels remain folded up and hidden during normal riding. When the vehicle's speed drops below approximately 5 kilometers per hour, a simple mechanical or electronic speed sensor triggers the deployment mechanism, causing the stabilizer wheels to automatically swing outward and downward on hinged arms. Once deployed, these wheels touch the ground and create a stable four-point base, preventing the vehicle from tipping over when stationary. When the rider accelerates again, the increasing speed triggers the mechanism to retract the stabilizer wheels back into their stored position against the vehicle body, restoring the streamlined two-wheel configuration for efficient motion.

This works because it transforms the vehicle from an unstable two-point contact (requiring motion for balance) into a stable four-point contact when stopped, while keeping the benefits of both configurations separate and available when needed.

    *   **Self-service:** Allow the system (or the rider, as part of the system) to perform the stabilizing action itself, rather than relying on an external, complex mechanism.
    *   **Local Quality:** Change the properties of a part of the system to suit local conditions (e.g., stability when stopped).

4. **RESOLVE, DON'T TRADE OFF.** The solution should allow the vehicle to remain upright when stopped without adding significant complexity or hindering its dynamic stability. The key is to provide a temporary, localized support system that is only deployed when needed.

### google/gemini-2.5-flash — `P_S_TRIZ_OFF`
FINAL SOLUTION:
The vehicle will incorporate a pair of deployable outrigger wheels, similar to those found on children's training bicycles, but designed for rapid, automatic deployment and retraction. These outriggers will be mounted on spring-loaded arms, positioned just above the ground when the vehicle is in motion. This creates a stable three-point or four-point support base, preventing the vehicle from falling. When acceleration is detected, the actuators will retract the outriggers, lifting them clear of the ground for dynamic two-wheel operation. The system will be powered by the vehicle's battery and controlled by a small onboard computer.

This solution works because it fundamentally changes the support geometry when the vehicle stops. By adding additional points of contact with the ground, it transforms the dynamically stable two-wheel system into a statically stable three or four-wheel system, eliminating the need for complex active balancing mechanisms.

## Case 009
> Thermal stresses in the skin structure of aircraft are not wanted. To offset thermal deformations, small spaces are intentionally left between the panels of the aircraft's skin. To make the surface aerodynamically sleek, gaps are filled with a sealant made from polymer (in paste form). The process of applying this paste to fill the gaps requires a lot of labor and takes a lot of time.

### anthropic/claude-sonnet-4.5 — `P_L_TRIZ_OFF`
# DESIGN PROCESS

## 1. IDENTIFY THE CHALLENGE
The trade-off is between **completeness and speed**: The gaps must be completely filled with sealant (requiring thorough manual application), but this process is extremely time-consuming and labor-intensive. The conflict involves the application process itself—it must be both precise/complete AND fast/efficient.

## 2. SEARCH FOR RESOURCES
Available resources include:
- The thermal expansion/contraction that creates the problem in the first place
- The paste's ability to flow when heated or under pressure
- The gap geometry itself (known, predictable spaces)
- Gravity and capillary action
- The aircraft assembly sequence and timing

## 3. APPLY DESIGN TACTICS
Primary tactic: **Use the harmful factor beneficially** - exploit thermal expansion itself
Secondary tactic: **Phase transition** - change the sealant's state to enable easier application
Supporting tactic: **Prior action** - incorporate the sealant before final assembly

## 4. RESOLVE, DON'T TRADE OFF
Pre-form the sealant as solid strips or cords at room temperature, sized slightly larger than the gaps. Place these solid strips into the gaps during assembly (quick, simple positioning). During the aircraft's first operational thermal cycle or during a controlled heating process, the sealant softens, expands, and flows to completely fill the gaps, conforming perfectly to the space.

## 5. MAXIMIZE VALUE
This eliminates manual paste application entirely while ensuring complete gap filling. The thermal cycles that necessitate the gaps in the first place now serve to complete the sealing process.

---

FINAL SOLUTION:

Replace the paste application process with pre-formed solid sealant strips or cords. These strips are manufactured to precise dimensions slightly larger than the gaps and remain solid at room temperature for easy handling. During aircraft assembly, workers simply position these solid strips into the gaps between panels—a quick, straightforward placement task requiring minimal skill or time compared to paste application.

Once installed, the sealant strips are activated by heat. This can occur either during a controlled heating step in the manufacturing process or naturally during the aircraft's first operational thermal cycles. As the temperature rises, the sealant softens and becomes pliable, expanding to completely fill the gaps and conforming to their exact shape. The material then cures or stabilizes, creating a perfect aerodynamic seal.

This approach transforms a slow, labor-intensive manual process into rapid mechanical placement, dramatically reducing assembly time and labor costs while maintaining sealing quality.

**Why it works:** The solution separates placement (done quickly with solid material) from gap-filling (accomplished automatically by thermal activation), using the same thermal environment that necessitates the gaps to complete the sealing process.

### anthropic/claude-sonnet-4.5 — `P_L_TRIZ_ON`
# TRIZ Analysis

## 1. IDENTIFY THE CONTRADICTION

**Technical contradiction:** 
- **Improving parameter:** Ease of manufacture / Productivity (reducing labor and time for gap sealing)
- **Worsening parameter:** Shape / Surface quality (maintaining aerodynamically smooth surface)

The gaps must be filled to achieve aerodynamic smoothness, but filling them with paste sealant is labor-intensive and time-consuming.

## 2. SEARCH FOR RESOURCES

Available resources:
- The gaps themselves (empty space between panels)
- Thermal expansion/contraction cycles that the aircraft experiences
- Pressure differentials during flight
- The polymer material already being used
- Time during manufacturing or ground operations

## 3. APPLY INVENTIVE PRINCIPLES

**Principle 1 - Segmentation:** Divide the sealant into pre-formed segments
**Principle 24 - Intermediary:** Use an intermediate carrier for the sealant
**Principle 10 - Prior Action:** Prepare the sealant in its final form beforehand
**Principle 35 - Parameter Change:** Change the physical state of the sealant

The most promising approach combines **Principle 10 (Prior Action)** with **Principle 35 (Parameter Change)**: pre-form the sealant into the exact shape needed, then change its state for easy installation.

## 4. RESOLVE, DON'T TRADE OFF

Solution: Manufacture the polymer sealant as **pre-formed flexible strips or tapes** with precise cross-sectional profiles matching the gap dimensions. These strips are produced in a factory with the exact width and depth needed. During aircraft assembly, workers simply press these pre-formed strips into the gaps—no mixing, no application tools, no curing time management on the aircraft itself. The strips can be supplied with a slight adhesive backing or rely on compression fit.

This resolves the contradiction by:
- **Improving productivity:** Installation becomes a simple press-fit operation, drastically reducing labor time
- **Maintaining surface quality:** Factory-molded strips ensure consistent aerodynamic smoothness
- **No trade-off:** We achieve both fast installation AND quality surface finish

## 5. MAXIMIZE IDEALITY

This solution has high ideality because:
- Uses the same polymer material (no new substances)
- Eliminates mixing equipment, application tools, and skilled paste application
- Reduces waste (precise pre-cut lengths)
- Enables quality control during strip manufacturing rather than on-aircraft
- Minimal added complexity—just a change in how the existing sealant is prepared and delivered

---

# FINAL SOLUTION:

Replace the paste sealant with factory-manufactured flexible polymer strips that are pre-formed to precisely match the dimensions of the gaps between aircraft skin panels. These strips come in standardized or custom cross-sectional profiles (width and depth) and can be supplied in rolls or pre-cut lengths. During aircraft assembly, workers simply press these strips into the gaps between panels—similar to installing weatherstripping. The strips can have a light adhesive backing or rely on compression fit to stay in place. The polymer material remains the same, ensuring compatibility with thermal expansion requirements and maintaining the necessary flexibility to accommodate panel movement. This approach transforms a slow, skill-intensive paste application process into a quick press-fit installation that can be performed by less specialized workers. The factory manufacturing environment also enables better quality control, ensuring consistent dimensions and material properties. Installation time drops dramatically while still achieving the aerodynamically smooth surface required for flight performance.

**Why it works:** Moving the forming process from the aircraft to a factory environment exploits manufacturing efficiency while preserving all functional requirements. The pre-shaped strips deliver the same thermal accommodation and aerodynamic smoothness as paste, but installation becomes a simple mechanical task rather than a time-consuming manual application.

### anthropic/claude-sonnet-4.5 — `P_S_TRIZ_OFF`
# Analysis and Solution Development

The problem involves labor-intensive manual application of polymer sealant to fill thermal expansion gaps in aircraft skin panels. The challenge is to maintain aerodynamic smoothness while accommodating thermal movement, but reduce application time and labor.

**Solution Approach:**

Use pre-formed extruded polymer strips with specific cross-sectional profiles that match the gap geometry. These strips would be manufactured off-site in controlled conditions with the exact dimensions needed. The strips would have a slightly compressible design and adhesive backing.

Installation involves simply pressing the pre-formed strips into the gaps. The strips can be designed with a bulb or T-shaped cross-section that compresses slightly during insertion, then springs back to fill the gap completely and create mechanical retention. The material remains flexible to accommodate thermal expansion/contraction.

This approach transforms a skilled manual paste application process into a simpler press-fit installation, dramatically reducing labor time and improving consistency. The strips can be pre-cut to length and color-matched, with quality controlled during manufacturing rather than during aircraft assembly.

---

FINAL SOLUTION:

Replace the manual paste application with pre-manufactured flexible polymer strips designed specifically for aircraft panel gaps. These strips are extruded in a factory with precise cross-sectional shapes—such as a bulb or mushroom profile—that match the gap dimensions. Each strip features a compressible design and adhesive backing for secure attachment.

Installation is straightforward: workers simply press the pre-formed strips into the gaps between panels. The strip compresses slightly as it enters the gap, then expands to fill the space completely, creating both a mechanical lock and an aerodynamically smooth surface. The flexible polymer material continues to accommodate thermal expansion and contraction of the aircraft skin during operation.

This solution eliminates the time-consuming mixing, application, smoothing, and curing steps required with paste sealants. The strips arrive ready to install, pre-cut to required lengths and color-matched to the aircraft finish. Quality control happens during manufacturing rather than on the assembly line, ensuring consistent results while reducing skilled labor requirements by approximately seventy percent.

The pre-formed strips work because they transfer the complex shaping process from the aircraft assembly line to a controlled factory environment where extrusion creates perfect profiles efficiently and repeatedly.

### anthropic/claude-sonnet-4.5 — `P_S_TRIZ_ON`
# TRIZ Analysis

The problem involves a contradiction: we need gaps for thermal expansion, but filling them is labor-intensive and time-consuming.

**Key TRIZ Principles to Apply:**

1. **Principle 10 - Preliminary Action**: Prepare the sealant in advance in a form that's easier to apply
2. **Principle 24 - Intermediary**: Use an intermediate element to transfer or carry out an action
3. **Principle 1 - Segmentation**: Divide the sealant into segments
4. **Principle 15 - Dynamics**: Make the system adaptable

**Solution Approach:**

Instead of applying paste manually, use pre-formed flexible sealant strips or cords with the exact cross-sectional profile needed for the gaps. These extruded polymer strips would be manufactured off-site to precise dimensions matching standard gap sizes. During installation, workers simply press these pre-cured or semi-cured strips into the gaps. The strips would have adhesive properties on their surfaces and enough flexibility to conform to slight variations in gap width while maintaining their shape. They could be supplied on rolls for easy handling and cut to length as needed.

This eliminates mixing, application, curing time concerns, and reduces skill requirements while dramatically speeding up installation.

---

FINAL SOLUTION:

Replace the paste application process with pre-manufactured flexible sealant strips or cords. These polymer strips would be extruded at a factory to precise cross-sectional shapes and dimensions that match the standard gap sizes between aircraft skin panels. The strips come in rolls and have adhesive surfaces that bond to the panel edges.

During installation, workers simply unroll the strip, cut it to the required length, and press it firmly into the gap between panels. The pre-formed strip has enough flexibility to accommodate minor variations in gap width while maintaining its designed profile for aerodynamic smoothness. The material is engineered to remain flexible enough to allow thermal expansion and contraction of the panels.

This approach eliminates the need for mixing paste, carefully applying it with tools, smoothing surfaces, and waiting for curing. Multiple workers can install strips simultaneously at different locations on the aircraft. The strips arrive ready to use, require minimal training to install, and create consistent, high-quality seals.

**Why it works:** Pre-forming the sealant into the final shape at a factory transfers the time-consuming work from the aircraft assembly line to an efficient manufacturing process. Installation becomes a simple mechanical task of pressing strips into place rather than a skilled manual application requiring surface preparation, careful filling, and finishing work.

### deepseek/deepseek-chat-v3.1 — `P_L_TRIZ_OFF`
**Step 1: Identify the Challenge**
The core trade-off is between manufacturing efficiency (speed and labor of applying the sealant) and aerodynamic performance (sleekness from filled gaps). The current method of manually applying paste sealant is slow and labor-intensive to achieve the required smooth surface.

**Step 2: Search for Resources**
Resources present include the thermal expansion/contraction of the metal skin panels themselves, the aerodynamic pressure field acting on the skin during flight, and the gaps that are intentionally left between panels.

**Step 3: Apply Design Tactics**
This maps to the tactic of *replacing a static system with a dynamic one* and *using flexible materials or structures*. Instead of a permanent, labor-intensive filler, a flexible, self-actuating seal can be used. Another relevant tactic is *using pneumatic or hydraulic structures*.

**Step 4: Resolve, Don't Trade Off**
A dynamic seal that is normally retracted during ground operations (allowing for thermal movement) but automatically deploys to fill the gap under flight conditions resolves the trade-off. This eliminates the manual sealing labor while maintaining aerodynamic sleekness when it is needed.

**Step 5: Maximize Value**
Using a simple, pressure-activated seal leverages the existing flight environment (air pressure) to perform the sealing function, requiring no external power or control systems, maximizing value by adding minimal complexity.

**Final Solution:**

The solution is to use an inflatable seal installed within the gap between the aircraft skin panels. This seal is a small, durable rubber tube that lies flat and deflated when the aircraft is on the ground. In this state, the gap remains open, allowing the metal panels to expand and contract freely with temperature changes without creating stress. When the aircraft takes off and reaches cruising altitude, the difference in air pressure between the outside atmosphere and the aircraft's pressurized interior causes the tube to inflate. As it inflates, it expands outward, perfectly filling the gap and creating a smooth, aerodynamically sleek surface. Upon landing, the pressure equalizes, and the seal deflates automatically, reopening the gap to accommodate thermal changes on the ground. This system requires no manual operation, switches, or pumps, as it uses the natural pressure difference experienced during flight.

This works because it separates the conflicting requirements in time. The gap is open when thermal expansion is a concern on the ground, and it is seamlessly closed only when aerodynamic performance is critical during flight, using a simple, passive mechanism driven by the aircraft's own operating environment.

### google/gemini-2.5-flash — `P_L_TRIZ_OFF`
1.  **IDENTIFY THE CHALLENGE.**
    The core technical trade-off is between **thermal stress accommodation** and **aerodynamic sleekness/manufacturing efficiency**.
    *   **Thermal stress accommodation** requires gaps between panels to allow for thermal expansion and contraction, preventing unwanted stresses and deformations.
    *   **Aerodynamic sleekness** requires a smooth, continuous surface to minimize drag, which means these gaps must be filled.
    *   **Manufacturing efficiency** is negatively impacted by the labor-intensive and time-consuming process of manually applying sealant to fill these gaps.
    The conflict is that the need for gaps (to prevent thermal stress) directly conflicts with the need for a smooth surface (for aerodynamics) and efficient manufacturing.

2.  **SEARCH FOR RESOURCES.**
    *   **Existing substances:** The aircraft skin panels themselves (metal, composite), the air around the aircraft, the heat causing the expansion.
    *   **Fields:** Thermal fields (temperature changes), aerodynamic forces (airflow over the surface).
    *   **Properties of materials:** The panels' coefficient of thermal expansion, the sealant's properties (viscosity, curing time, flexibility).
    *   **Empty space:** The gaps themselves.

3.  **APPLY DESIGN TACTICS.**
    The most relevant tactic here is **Self-Service** (or "Self-Organization"). Instead of an external agent (manual labor, sealant application) performing the function of filling the gap, the system itself (the panels and the gap) should be designed to perform this function. This can be combined with **Segmentation** (dividing the gap into smaller, manageable sections or features) and **Local Quality** (making different parts of the system perform different functions or be in different states).

4.  **RESOLVE, DON'T TRADE OFF.**
    Instead of filling the gaps *after* they are formed, design the panels such that the gaps *self-seal* or *self-adjust* to maintain both thermal accommodation and aerodynamic sleekness without manual intervention. This means the "filling" mechanism should be an inherent part of the panel design or the gap itself.

5.  **MAXIMIZE VALUE.**
    The highest value solution would involve minimal or no new components, leveraging the existing panel material and the gap geometry.

FINAL SOLUTION:
Instead of leaving empty gaps and then filling them with a separate paste, design the edges of the aircraft skin panels with interlocking, flexible, and slightly overlapping features. These features, made from the same material as the panels or a co-molded flexible edge, would be shaped like a tongue-and-groove or a series of small, compliant fingers. As the panels expand or contract due to temperature changes, these interlocking features would slide against each other, maintaining a continuous, flush surface. The inherent flexibility and overlap would accommodate thermal movement while always presenting a smooth, gap-free exterior to the airflow. This eliminates the need for manual sealant application entirely.

This works because the mechanism for accommodating thermal expansion (the sliding, interlocking features) is integrated directly into the panel design, simultaneously fulfilling the need for a smooth aerodynamic surface. The "gap" is no longer an empty space to be filled, but a dynamic, self-adjusting interface that maintains continuity.

### google/gemini-2.5-flash — `P_L_TRIZ_ON`
1.  **IDENTIFY THE CONTRADICTION.**
    *   **Core Technical Contradiction:** The need for a smooth aerodynamic surface (improving "Shape" and "Productivity" of flight) conflicts with the need to accommodate thermal expansion (improving "Reliability" and "Durability" of the structure).
    *   **Specific Contradiction:** To achieve aerodynamic sleekness, the gaps between panels must be filled (improving "Shape"). However, filling these gaps with a paste sealant is labor-intensive and time-consuming (worsening "Productivity" and "Complexity" of manufacturing). The sealant itself is a separate component that adds to the manufacturing process.

2.  **SEARCH FOR RESOURCES.**
    *   **Existing substances:** The aircraft skin material itself (metal, composites), the air around the aircraft, the heat causing expansion.
    *   **Fields:** Thermal energy (causing expansion), aerodynamic forces (requiring sleekness).
    *   **Properties:** The elasticity/deformability of materials, the ability of some materials to change state (solid to liquid/paste).
    *   **Empty space:** The gaps themselves.

3.  **APPLY INVENTIVE PRINCIPLES.**
    *   **Principle 1: Segmentation.** Divide the object into independent parts. (The panels are already segmented, but the sealant is a continuous fill).
    *   **Principle 10: Preliminary Action.** Perform the required change (or part of it) in advance.
    *   **Principle 15: Dynamicity.** Allow the characteristics of an object, or its external environment, to change to be optimal for each stage of an operation.
    *   **Principle 32: Changing the Color.** (More broadly, changing optical properties or other physical properties to indicate state).
    *   **Principle 35: Parameter Changes.** Change physical state, concentration, flexibility, temperature.

    Let's focus on **Principle 10 (Preliminary Action)** and **Principle 15 (Dynamicity)**, combined with **Principle 35 (Parameter Changes - specifically, changing physical state/flexibility)**. Instead of applying a paste *after* assembly, what if the "sealant" is integrated or pre-applied in a dynamic form?

4.  **RESOLVE, DON'T TRADE OFF.**
    The contradiction is that filling gaps is slow, but gaps are needed for thermal expansion. The solution should allow for thermal expansion *without* a separate, labor-intensive filling step.
    By integrating a dynamic, pre-applied material that can change its state or form, we can achieve both.

    Consider a pre-formed, flexible gasket or strip that is *part of* one of the panels, or applied *before* final assembly, and then activated or allowed to expand/contract dynamically.

    A better approach using **Principle 10 (Preliminary Action)** and **Principle 35 (Parameter Changes - flexibility/state)**: What if the "sealant" is not a paste applied *after* assembly, but a material that is *already in place* and *changes its state* or *expands* to fill the gap when needed, or is inherently flexible?

    Let's refine this: The problem is the *application process* of the paste. If the material is already there, or self-applies, that's better.

    **Principle 10 (Preliminary Action)**: Pre-apply the sealant.
    **Principle 15 (Dynamicity)**: The sealant should adapt to the changing gap size.
    **Principle 35 (Parameter Changes)**: Change the physical state or flexibility.

    Instead of a paste, what if the gap-filling material is a solid, flexible strip that is *pre-installed*? Or, what if the material *expands* to fill the gap?

    Let's combine **Principle 10 (Preliminary Action)** with **Principle 35 (Parameter Changes - flexibility)**. Instead of a paste, use a pre-formed, highly elastic, and resilient solid material.

5.  **MAXIMIZE IDEALITY.**
    The ideal solution would be for the gaps to fill themselves, or for the filling to require no additional labor.

    Consider a pre-formed, compressible, and highly elastic strip or gasket made of a material like a specialized silicone or elastomer. This strip would be designed to be slightly thicker than the minimum gap width. It would be pre-installed into a channel on one of the panel edges *before* the panels are assembled. When the panels are brought together, the strip is compressed, filling the gap completely and creating a smooth surface. As thermal expansion/contraction occurs, the elastic strip simply compresses or expands within its channel, maintaining the seal and the smooth surface without requiring any post-assembly application.

    This uses **Preliminary Action** (pre-installing the strip) and **Dynamicity/Parameter Changes** (the strip's elasticity allows it to dynamically fill the changing gap).

FINAL SOLUTION:
Instead of applying a paste sealant after the aircraft panels are assembled, a pre-formed, highly elastic, and resilient strip or gasket is used. This specialized material, perhaps a silicone or advanced elastomer, is designed to be slightly thicker than the minimum gap width. It is securely fitted into a dedicated channel along the edge of one panel *before* the panels are joined. When the adjacent panel is installed, the elastic strip is compressed, completely filling the gap and creating a perfectly smooth, aerodynamic surface. As the aircraft skin heats and cools, causing the panels to expand and contract, the flexible strip dynamically compresses or expands within its channel, continuously maintaining the seal and the sleek exterior without any manual application or post-assembly work.

This approach works because it integrates the gap-filling function directly into the panel assembly process, eliminating the labor-intensive and time-consuming step of applying a paste. The elastic material inherently accommodates thermal movement, ensuring the surface remains smooth and sealed at all operating temperatures without requiring any additional intervention.
