## Case 013
> Eyeglass wearers need lenses that are clear and fully transmissive indoors and in dim light, yet dark enough to shield the eyes from glare and bright sun outdoors. A single fixed tint cannot do both: a clear lens gives no outdoor protection, while a permanently dark lens makes indoor vision poor. The very same lens must be light when its surroundings are dim and dark when its surroundings are bright.

### anthropic/claude-sonnet-4.5 — `P_L_TRIZ_OFF`
# DESIGN PROCESS

## 1. IDENTIFY THE CHALLENGE
The core trade-off is between **light transmission** and **protection from brightness**. A lens optimized for indoor use (high transmission, clear) fails outdoors (no glare protection), while a lens optimized for outdoor use (low transmission, dark) fails indoors (poor visibility). The same lens element must simultaneously be both transparent AND opaque depending on ambient light conditions—an internal design tension.

## 2. SEARCH FOR RESOURCES
Available resources include:
- The UV radiation present in outdoor sunlight but absent indoors
- The lens material itself (typically plastic or glass)
- Photochemical reactions that can occur within materials
- The existing lens structure without adding separate mechanical systems

## 3. APPLY DESIGN TACTICS
I'll apply **"Make it self-service"** (let the system adapt automatically to conditions) combined with **"Use phase transitions"** (change material state reversibly). Specifically, embed photochromic molecules in the lens material that undergo reversible chemical reactions triggered by UV light intensity.

## 4. RESOLVE, DON'T TRADE OFF
Incorporate photochromic compounds (such as silver halides or organic molecules like naphthopyrans) directly into the lens polymer matrix. When UV photons strike these molecules outdoors, they absorb energy and reconfigure into a darker, light-absorbing form. Indoors, without UV exposure, thermal energy causes the molecules to revert to their clear, transparent form. The transition is continuous and automatic—the lens darkens proportionally to UV intensity.

## 5. MAXIMIZE VALUE
This solution adds only molecular-scale additives to existing lens material—no moving parts, no electronics, no user intervention, and no additional subsystems. It delivers both functions (clarity indoors, protection outdoors) using the environmental UV gradient itself as the control signal.

---

# FINAL SOLUTION:

Embed special light-sensitive molecules called photochromic compounds directly into the eyeglass lens material during manufacturing. These molecules exist in two reversible chemical forms: a clear, colorless state and a darkened, light-absorbing state. When the wearer goes outdoors, ultraviolet radiation from sunlight strikes the molecules and provides the energy needed to transform them into their dark configuration. The lens darkens within seconds to minutes, blocking glare and protecting the eyes. When the wearer returns indoors where UV levels are negligible, the molecules naturally relax back to their clear state through thermal energy from the surrounding environment, restoring full transparency for comfortable indoor vision. The darkness level adjusts automatically and continuously based on UV intensity—brighter sun produces darker lenses, while dim conditions keep them clear.

This works because the solution uses the environmental condition itself (UV light) as both the trigger and the power source for the change, requiring no external control system. The molecules perform the switching function at the molecular level within the existing lens structure.

### anthropic/claude-sonnet-4.5 — `P_L_TRIZ_ON`
# TRIZ Analysis

## 1. IDENTIFY THE CONTRADICTION

**Physical contradiction:** The lens must be both transparent (to allow full light transmission indoors/dim conditions) AND opaque/dark (to block excessive light outdoors/bright conditions) at the same time.

In TRIZ parameter terms: we need high "illumination intensity" (parameter related to light transmission) indoors but low illumination intensity (blocking) outdoors, yet it's the same physical lens.

## 2. SEARCH FOR RESOURCES

Available resources:
- The environmental light itself (the very thing causing the problem)
- UV radiation present in sunlight but absent indoors
- Chemical substances that can respond to light
- The lens material itself as a substrate
- Time (transition can occur over seconds/minutes)

## 3. APPLY INVENTIVE PRINCIPLES

**Principle #15 - Dynamics:** Make an object or environment automatically adapt to changing conditions.

**Principle #35 - Parameter Change:** Change the physical or chemical state of the system (reversibly change optical properties).

**Principle #25 - Self-service:** Make the object service itself by performing auxiliary helpful functions (the lens responds to the condition that creates the need).

## 4. RESOLVE, DON'T TRADE OFF

Embed photochromic molecules into the lens material. These molecules undergo a reversible chemical reaction when exposed to UV light: they change molecular structure to absorb visible light (darken), and revert when UV is removed (lighten). The environmental UV radiation—abundant outdoors, minimal indoors—directly triggers the state change needed.

This resolves the contradiction: the SAME lens is clear indoors (no UV → molecules in open form) and dark outdoors (UV present → molecules in closed, light-absorbing form).

## 5. MAXIMIZE IDEALITY

This solution has high ideality:
- Uses existing environmental resource (UV light) as the trigger
- No external power, sensors, or control systems needed
- No user intervention required
- Adds only molecular-level dopants to existing lens material
- The harmful element (bright sunlight with UV) itself activates the protection

---

# FINAL SOLUTION:

Embed special light-sensitive molecules called photochromic compounds uniformly throughout the lens material during manufacturing. These molecules exist in two reversible forms: a colorless "open" structure and a colored "closed" structure that absorbs visible light. When ultraviolet radiation—which is abundant in outdoor sunlight but largely absent in indoor artificial lighting—strikes the lens, it provides energy that causes the molecules to shift into their closed, darkened form within seconds. The lens becomes tinted, reducing glare and brightness. When the wearer moves indoors or into shade where UV levels drop, the molecules naturally relax back to their open, transparent form over a few minutes, restoring full clarity. The transition happens automatically and continuously, matching the lens darkness to the ambient UV intensity. Modern formulations use molecules like indolino-naphthospiropyrans or naphthopyrans that cycle reliably through thousands of transitions without degrading, maintaining performance for years of daily use.

**Why it works:** The solution exploits the environmental condition itself—UV radiation in bright outdoor light—as both the signal and the energy source to trigger the protective response, making the lens self-regulating without any added mechanisms.

### anthropic/claude-sonnet-4.5 — `P_S_TRIZ_OFF`
# Analysis

The problem requires lenses that automatically adapt their tint based on ambient light conditions - clear indoors and dark outdoors - without manual intervention or fixed tinting.

# Solution Approach

Use photochromic lenses that contain special light-sensitive molecules embedded in the lens material. These molecules undergo a reversible chemical reaction when exposed to ultraviolet (UV) radiation from sunlight.

**Detailed Solution:**

The lenses are made from optical-quality plastic or glass infused with photochromic compounds such as silver halides or organic molecules like naphthopyrans. In their default state indoors (minimal UV exposure), these molecules maintain an open-ring chemical structure that allows visible light to pass through freely, keeping the lens clear.

When the wearer steps outdoors, UV rays from sunlight trigger the molecules to rearrange into a closed-ring structure that absorbs visible light, causing the lens to darken within 30-60 seconds. The degree of darkening is proportional to UV intensity - brighter sun produces darker tint.

Upon returning indoors where UV levels drop, the molecules gradually revert to their original clear state over 2-5 minutes. The process is fully reversible and can repeat thousands of times throughout the lens lifetime. Modern formulations can achieve 80-90% light transmission indoors and block 75-85% of light outdoors.

---

# FINAL SOLUTION:

The eyeglass lenses contain special light-sensitive molecules embedded throughout the lens material. These molecules automatically change their structure when exposed to ultraviolet light from the sun. Indoors, where there is little UV light, the molecules stay in a form that lets light pass through easily, keeping the lenses clear for normal vision. When the wearer goes outside, the UV rays cause these molecules to shift into a different arrangement that absorbs visible light, making the lenses darken to protect against glare and brightness. The darker the sunlight, the more molecules activate, so the tint adjusts to conditions. When returning indoors, the absence of UV light allows the molecules to gradually return to their clear state within a few minutes. This chemical transformation is completely reversible and can happen thousands of times without wearing out. The lenses thus provide clear vision in dim environments while automatically offering sun protection in bright conditions, all within the same pair of glasses.

**Why it works:** The photochromic molecules act as automatic light valves, responding directly to the environmental trigger (UV radiation) that correlates with brightness, creating a self-regulating system that darkens in bright conditions and clears in dim ones.

### anthropic/claude-sonnet-4.5 — `P_S_TRIZ_ON`
# TRIZ Analysis

The contradiction here is that the lens must be both transparent (indoors) and opaque/dark (outdoors) - opposite optical states. This is a physical contradiction where the same parameter (light transmission) must have opposite values under different conditions.

**TRIZ Principles Applied:**
- **Principle 15 (Dynamics)**: Make the object adaptive, changing characteristics based on conditions
- **Principle 28 (Mechanics Substitution)**: Replace mechanical means with optical/chemical fields
- **Principle 35 (Parameter Changes)**: Change the physical/chemical state of the system

**Solution Approach:**
Embed photochromic molecules into the lens material. These molecules undergo a reversible chemical reaction when exposed to UV light, changing their molecular structure and thus their light absorption properties. In bright sunlight (high UV), the molecules transform to an activated state that absorbs visible light, darkening the lens. Indoors or in dim light (low UV), the molecules revert to their transparent state.

This uses the environmental condition itself (UV radiation) as the trigger for the state change, creating a self-regulating system that automatically adapts to lighting conditions.

---

# FINAL SOLUTION:

Embed special light-sensitive molecules called photochromic compounds directly into the eyeglass lens material during manufacturing. These molecules have a remarkable property: they automatically and reversibly change their structure when exposed to ultraviolet (UV) radiation from sunlight.

When you step outdoors into bright sunlight, the UV rays trigger a chemical transformation in these molecules, causing them to rearrange into a form that absorbs visible light. This makes the entire lens darken within seconds, providing protection from glare and brightness. The more intense the UV exposure, the darker the lenses become.

When you return indoors or into dim lighting where UV levels are minimal, the molecules naturally revert to their original transparent structure. The lenses gradually clear up, typically within a few minutes, restoring full visibility for indoor conditions.

This works because the photochromic molecules act as automatic sensors and responders to the ambient light environment. The UV radiation itself provides the energy needed to drive the molecular transformation, creating a self-powered, adaptive system that requires no batteries, switches, or user intervention to transition between clear and dark states.

The intensity of the UV light dictates the degree of darkening; stronger sunlight causes a darker tint. When the UV light source is removed, such as when moving indoors, a reverse chemical reaction occurs. The molecules revert to their original transparent form, allowing the lens to clear. The speed of this transition is governed by thermal energy, so it happens faster in warmer conditions.

This works because it directly links the lens property (darkness) to the environmental trigger (sunlight intensity) via a reversible photochemical reaction. The molecules act as automatic, passive sensors and actuators, requiring no power or user input. The system leverages the fundamental difference between indoor and outdoor light—the presence of significant ultraviolet radiation—to drive the change, ensuring the lens is only dark when necessary for protection.

FINAL SOLUTION:

The solution is to create eyeglass lenses that contain millions of tiny, light-sensitive molecules permanently embedded within the plastic or glass. These special molecules are completely clear and colorless when you are indoors or in the shade. However, when you go outside into bright sunlight, the invisible ultraviolet rays from the sun cause a chemical change in these molecules. They temporarily change their structure and begin to absorb visible light, causing the entire lens to darken, much like a pair of sunglasses. The brighter the sun, the darker the lenses become. When you go back inside, away from the sun's ultraviolet light, the chemical change reverses itself. The molecules gradually return to their original, clear state, making the lenses transparent again for clear vision in dimmer conditions. This entire process is automatic and continuous, providing the right level of tint for your surroundings without any buttons or switches.

## Case 016
> Liquid medicine is packaged in small glass ampoules whose necks are fused shut by a flame to give a perfectly airtight seal. The heat needed to melt and close the glass also reaches the medicine just below the neck, and many of these formulations are heat-sensitive and begin to degrade at those temperatures. We need a hermetic flame seal without letting the sealing heat spoil the medicine inside.

### anthropic/claude-sonnet-4.5 — `P_L_TRIZ_OFF`
# DESIGN PROCESS

## 1. IDENTIFY THE CHALLENGE
The core trade-off: We need high temperature at the glass neck (to melt and seal it) but low temperature at the medicine (to prevent degradation). This is an internal design tension in the thermal distribution — the system must be both hot (for sealing) and cold (for medicine protection) simultaneously.

## 2. SEARCH FOR RESOURCES
Available resources include:
- The liquid medicine itself (thermal mass, heat capacity)
- The ampoule geometry (neck is physically separated from the body)
- Time (we can control when and how long heat is applied)
- The surrounding environment (air, potential coolants)
- Phase change properties (evaporation absorbs heat)
- Gravity (liquid naturally settles at bottom)

## 3. APPLY DESIGN TACTICS
Primary tactic: **Segmentation in space** — physically separate the hot zone from the cold zone by maximizing distance and minimizing thermal conduction path.

Secondary tactic: **Local quality** — create different thermal conditions in different parts of the system simultaneously.

Supporting tactic: **Prior counteraction** — pre-cool the medicine before sealing to create a thermal buffer.

## 4. RESOLVE, DON'T TRADE OFF
**Solution approach:** Invert the ampoule during sealing so the medicine pools at the bottom (away from the neck), then actively cool the medicine-containing portion while flame-sealing the neck. Use a focused, rapid flame (oxy-hydrogen torch) that melts only the narrow neck zone in 1-2 seconds, combined with a cooling jacket or cold gas jet directed at the ampoule body.

The physical implementation:
- Mount ampoule inverted in a fixture
- Apply a water-cooled metal collar or directed CO₂/nitrogen gas stream to the lower body where medicine sits
- Use a small, intense flame (oxy-hydrogen or plasma) focused precisely on the neck
- Complete seal in under 2 seconds
- The thermal gradient is maintained: neck reaches 1000°C+ while medicine stays below 40°C

## 5. MAXIMIZE VALUE
This solution adds minimal equipment (cooling collar/gas nozzle, better torch) to existing sealing stations. It exploits gravity (free resource) and the ampoule's existing geometry. No redesign of the ampoule itself is needed, and the process remains fast and scalable for production lines.

---

# FINAL SOLUTION:

Invert the ampoule during the sealing process so gravity pulls the liquid medicine away from the neck and into the wider body at the bottom. While the ampoule is upside down, wrap the lower portion (where the medicine now sits) with a water-cooled metal collar or direct a stream of cold carbon dioxide or nitrogen gas at it to actively remove heat. Simultaneously, apply a small, intensely focused flame—such as an oxy-hydrogen torch—to the narrow neck for only 1-2 seconds. This rapid, localized heating melts and fuses the glass neck to create the hermetic seal while the cooling system prevents heat from conducting down to the medicine. The combination of physical separation (medicine far from the heat source), active cooling at the medicine location, and ultra-fast sealing keeps the liquid below its degradation temperature throughout the process.

**Why it works:** The inverted orientation uses gravity to maximize distance between the heat source and the medicine. Active cooling at the medicine location removes any conducted heat before it can accumulate, while the focused, brief flame application limits total heat input. These three elements together maintain a steep thermal gradient—hot neck, cold medicine—resolving the temperature conflict without compromise.

### anthropic/claude-sonnet-4.5 — `P_L_TRIZ_ON`
# TRIZ Analysis

## 1. IDENTIFY THE CONTRADICTION

**Technical contradiction:** We need to increase temperature (to melt glass for hermetic sealing) while simultaneously keeping temperature low (to preserve heat-sensitive medicine). 

In TRIZ parameters: Temperature of the sealing zone must be high, but temperature of the medicine must remain low.

This is also a **physical contradiction**: The neck region must be hot (to seal) AND cold (to protect contents).

## 2. SEARCH FOR RESOURCES

Available resources:
- The medicine itself (liquid, thermal mass)
- The ampoule geometry (long neck separating seal point from liquid)
- Time dimension (sequence of operations)
- Gravity and liquid properties
- Cold substances/fields in the environment
- The glass material's thermal conductivity

## 3. APPLY INVENTIVE PRINCIPLES

**Principle 1 - Segmentation in time**: Separate the conflicting requirements temporally.

**Principle 24 - Intermediary**: Use an intermediate substance or process.

**Principle 10 - Prior action**: Prepare the system in advance to counteract harmful effects.

**Combination approach**: Freeze the medicine before sealing, creating a thermal buffer that absorbs heat conducted down from the flame-sealed neck.

## 4. RESOLVE, DON'T TRADE OFF

Solution: Before flame-sealing, freeze the medicine inside the ampoule to well below 0°C (e.g., -20°C to -40°C using dry ice or a freezer). The frozen medicine acts as a massive heat sink. When the neck is flame-sealed, any heat conducted downward is absorbed by the phase-change process (melting ice) and by raising the temperature of the cold mass, keeping the medicine well below its degradation temperature throughout the sealing operation. The seal completes before significant heat reaches the still-cold medicine.

## 5. MAXIMIZE IDEALITY

This solution adds no new components to the ampoule itself—it uses only the medicine's existing thermal properties and a preparatory cooling step. No complex equipment modifications, no additional materials in the final product, and no compromise in seal quality or medicine protection.

---

FINAL SOLUTION:

Before sealing each ampoule, freeze the liquid medicine inside to approximately -20°C to -40°C using a standard freezer or dry ice bath. The frozen medicine becomes a large thermal reservoir that absorbs heat. When the glass neck is heated with a flame to create the hermetic seal, any heat that conducts down the narrow neck toward the medicine is immediately absorbed by the frozen mass. The ice must first warm up and then melt before the liquid medicine itself can heat up, and this phase change alone consumes substantial thermal energy. By the time the flame sealing is complete—which takes only seconds—the medicine remains cold and protected. After sealing, the ampoule returns to room temperature naturally, with the medicine thawing undamaged inside its perfectly sealed container.

This works because the frozen medicine acts as a built-in heat sink, exploiting the high thermal capacity of ice and the energy-absorbing phase transition from solid to liquid to protect the heat-sensitive formulation during the brief sealing operation.

### anthropic/claude-sonnet-4.5 — `P_S_TRIZ_OFF`
# Analysis and Solution Development

The core challenge is protecting heat-sensitive medicine from the thermal energy required to create a hermetic glass seal at the ampoule neck.

**Solution Approach:**

Create a thermal barrier by freezing the medicine before flame-sealing. Fill the ampoule with medicine, leaving appropriate headspace. Rapidly freeze the liquid contents using liquid nitrogen or a cryogenic bath to create a solid ice plug. This frozen mass acts as a heat sink and thermal barrier. The ice layer nearest the neck absorbs the sealing heat through its latent heat of fusion and specific heat capacity, preventing temperature rise in the bulk medicine below. 

Immediately flame-seal the neck while contents remain frozen. The glass melts and fuses at ~1000°C, but the frozen medicine just millimeters away stays well below its degradation temperature. After sealing, allow controlled thawing at room temperature or in a temperature-controlled environment.

The frozen buffer provides sufficient thermal mass and distance to prevent heat conduction from reaching critical levels in the medicine bulk before the seal completes.

---

FINAL SOLUTION:

Before sealing each ampoule, rapidly freeze the liquid medicine inside using liquid nitrogen or a cryogenic freezing bath. The medicine transforms into solid ice, creating a protective thermal barrier between the glass neck and the bulk of the product below. While the medicine remains frozen, quickly apply the flame to melt and fuse the glass neck shut, creating the hermetic seal. The frozen medicine acts as a heat sink, absorbing the thermal energy through the phase change from solid back to liquid and through its heat capacity. This prevents the sealing temperature from penetrating deeply into the ampoule contents. The ice layer closest to the neck may warm slightly, but the medicine just centimeters below stays cold and protected from degradation. After sealing is complete, the ampoules are allowed to thaw gradually under controlled conditions, returning the medicine to its liquid state without any heat damage having occurred during the critical sealing step.

**Why it works:** The frozen medicine provides a large thermal mass with high heat absorption capacity directly below the seal point, effectively blocking heat conduction and keeping the bulk medicine below its degradation temperature during the brief sealing process.

### anthropic/claude-sonnet-4.5 — `P_S_TRIZ_ON`
# TRIZ Analysis

The contradiction here is that we need high temperature to seal the glass (beneficial) but this same heat damages the medicine (harmful). This is a physical contradiction where heat is both needed and not needed in the same zone.

**Relevant TRIZ Principles:**
- **Principle 1 (Segmentation)**: Separate the sealing zone from the medicine
- **Principle 10 (Prior Action)**: Freeze the medicine before sealing
- **Principle 24 (Intermediary)**: Use a protective barrier
- **Principle 28 (Mechanics Substitution)**: Replace thermal process

**Solution Approach:**

Apply **Principle 10 (Prior Action)** combined with **Principle 1 (Segmentation)**: Before flame-sealing, freeze the medicine and the lower portion of the ampoule using a cryogenic collar or cold gas jet (liquid nitrogen or CO2). The frozen medicine acts as a heat sink, and the ice/frozen liquid creates a thermal barrier. The neck can then be flame-sealed while the medicine remains cold. The temperature gradient is steep enough that the sealing heat dissipates into the glass and surrounding cold zone rather than reaching the medicine.

---

FINAL SOLUTION:

Before sealing each ampoule with the flame, rapidly cool the medicine and the lower portion of the glass container using a collar that circulates extremely cold gas (such as liquid nitrogen vapor or carbon dioxide). This freezing step takes only seconds. Once the medicine is frozen solid, immediately apply the flame to seal the neck. The frozen medicine acts as a cold reservoir that absorbs any heat trying to travel downward from the sealing zone. The ice creates a thermal barrier, and the temperature drops sharply over a very short distance in the glass neck.

This approach works because frozen materials have high thermal mass and low thermal conductivity. The heat from the flame dissipates into the glass and the surrounding cold zone before it can travel down to warm and damage the medicine. By the time the medicine thaws naturally to room temperature, the sealing is complete and the glass neck has cooled, leaving the medicine unharmed and the seal perfectly airtight.

### deepseek/deepseek-chat-v3.1 — `P_L_TRIZ_OFF`
**Step 1: Identify the Challenge**  
The core trade-off is between achieving a hermetic seal (requiring high heat to melt the glass) and preserving the medicine (which degrades when heated). Increasing heat improves sealing but worsens thermal damage; reducing heat protects the medicine but prevents a proper seal.

**Step 2: Search for Resources**  
Available resources include the glass ampoule itself (a poor thermal conductor), the medicine (which may be liquid), and the ambient air. The sealing flame is an external energy source. The neck of the ampoule is thin and can be heated locally, but heat conducts downward.

**Step 3: Apply Design Tactics**  
The tactic of **thermal insulation** is relevant: isolate the heat-sensitive zone from the heat-application zone. Another is **localized heating**: apply heat only where needed. A third is **heat sinking**: draw heat away from the medicine using a material that absorbs thermal energy. Combining these, we can actively cool the body of the ampoule while heating the neck.

**Step 4: Resolve, Don't Trade Off**  
By cooling the lower part of the ampoule (e.g., with a chilled liquid or cold air jet) simultaneously with neck heating, the heat is conducted away from the medicine. The temperature gradient becomes steep: the neck melts for sealing, but the medicine stays cool.

**Step 5: Maximize Value**  
This uses simple, existing cooling methods (like a refrigeration unit or compressed air) added to the sealing station. It requires minimal new components and avoids altering the ampoule or medicine, maximizing reliability and simplicity.

**Why it works:**  
Active cooling of the ampoule body creates a steep thermal gradient. Heat from the flame conducts axially along the glass, but the cooling sink removes this energy before it reaches the medicine. The neck gets hot enough to melt and seal, while the lower portion remains at a safe temperature.

FINAL SOLUTION:

To seal the glass ampoule without overheating the medicine, the sealing process is modified to include simultaneous cooling of the ampoule body. As the flame heats the neck of the ampoule to melt the glass shut, a stream of chilled air or a cooled liquid bath contacts the lower part of the ampoule, well below the neck. This cooling acts as a heat sink, drawing thermal energy away from the medicine chamber. The glass in the neck becomes soft and fuses airtight, but the heat traveling down the glass is rapidly removed by the cooling mechanism. The temperature difference between the hot neck and the cool body becomes very sharp, so the medicine never reaches damaging temperatures. The cooling can be applied by a simple ring jet of cold air directed around the ampoule just below the shoulder, or by having the lower portion sit in a cooled metal block during sealing. This approach allows use of the standard flame sealing method but protects the heat-sensitive liquid inside by actively controlling the heat flow within the glass.

**Step 1 – Identify the Contradiction**  
The core technical contradiction is between **Temperature** (the heat needed to melt the glass for a hermetic seal) and **Reliability/Stability** (the medicine’s integrity, which degrades when heated).  
Alternatively, this can be framed as a physical contradiction: the glass neck must be **hot** (to melt and seal) and **cold** (to avoid heating the medicine below).

**Step 2 – Search for Resources**  
Resources present: the glass itself (can conduct or insulate heat), the medicine (liquid, possibly volatile), the flame (heat source), ambient air, and the geometry of the ampoule (neck vs. body). A key resource is the **temperature gradient** along the neck—heat travels downward by conduction.

**Step 3 – Apply Inventive Principles**  
Relevant TRIZ principles:  
- **Principle 2 – Taking out**: Remove the harmful part (heat) from the medicine zone.  
- **Principle 14 – Spheroidality/Curvature**: Use curved or spherical shapes to control heat flow.  
- **Principle 31 – Porous materials**: Use a porous or hollow insulator.  
- **Principle 35 – Parameter changes**: Change the physical/chemical properties of part of the system.  
- **Principle 4 – Asymmetry**: Change the shape to protect one side.  

Combining **Principle 2 (Taking out)** and **Principle 31 (Porous materials)** suggests **localized cooling or thermal insulation** right below the sealing zone to block heat from reaching the medicine.

**Step 4 – Resolve, Don’t Trade Off**  
Instead of reducing flame temperature (which risks a poor seal), we keep the sealing heat high but **confine it to the very tip of the neck** and actively remove or block conducted heat from traveling downward. This improves sealing reliability without degrading the medicine.

**Step 5 – Maximize Ideality**  
The most ideal solution uses existing system elements (glass, ambient air) and adds minimal complexity. A thin, temporary insulating collar or a focused cooling jet at the base of the neck exploits available resources to stop heat conduction, preserving medicine stability without altering the sealing process itself.

---

**FINAL SOLUTION:**

The solution is to add a narrow, removable cooling collar that fits snugly around the ampoule just below the neck during sealing. This collar is made of a material with high thermal conductivity, such as copper or aluminum, and contains internal channels through which a cool, harmless gas or liquid (like compressed air or chilled water) flows briefly. As the flame heats the very tip of the glass neck to melt and seal it, the collar actively draws heat away from the lower part of the neck through conduction and carries it off via the circulating coolant. This creates a sharp temperature gradient: the tip gets hot enough for a perfect glass fusion, while the medicine-filled body just millimeters below remains near room temperature.

Alternatively, a simpler version uses a disposable ring of phase-change material that wraps around the neck. This ring absorbs heat as it melts or sublimates, acting as a heat sink to protect the medicine. In either case, the collar or ring is removed after sealing, leaving no residue on the ampoule. The sealing process itself is unchanged—the flame still provides the exact heat needed for a hermetic glass seal—but the critical heat is prevented from traveling down into the medicine chamber.

This works because it physically interrupts the conduction path down the glass neck. Glass conducts heat, so without intervention, heat from the flame would spread downward and warm the medicine. The cooling collar acts as a thermal barrier, actively removing that conducted heat and localizing the high temperature to the very top where the seal forms. The medicine below stays cool, avoiding thermal degradation, while the seal remains strong and airtight.

## Case 022
> An elevator must raise a car loaded with passengers up many floors. If the hoist motor has to lift the entire dead weight of the car plus its load on every trip, it must be very powerful and it draws a large amount of energy each time it ascends, making the drive system big, expensive, and wasteful. We need to move the heavy car up and down without paying the full weight in motor size and energy on every trip.

### anthropic/claude-sonnet-4.5 — `P_L_TRIZ_OFF`
# DESIGN PROCESS

## 1. IDENTIFY THE CHALLENGE
The core trade-off is between lifting capability and energy/motor size. To lift the heavy car, we need a powerful motor that consumes large amounts of energy. But a powerful motor is expensive, bulky, and wasteful. The conflict: the system must handle heavy loads (requiring high power) while minimizing motor size and energy consumption (requiring low power).

## 2. SEARCH FOR RESOURCES
Key resource: **gravity itself and the weight of the descending car**. When the car goes down, gravity does work that is currently wasted. The car's mass on the way down is a resource we can exploit. Additionally, we have the elevator shaft space and the existing cable/pulley system.

## 3. APPLY DESIGN TACTICS
Primary tactic: **Counterweight** - use a balancing mass to offset the load. This applies the principle of converting harmful factors into useful ones, and using opposing forces to cancel each other out. The descending counterweight helps lift the ascending car, and vice versa.

## 4. RESOLVE, DON'T TRADE OFF
Install a counterweight equal to the car's empty weight plus approximately half the typical passenger load. Connect it via cables over pulleys so that as the car rises, the counterweight descends, and vice versa. The motor now only needs to overcome the difference between car+passengers and counterweight, not the full weight. This dramatically reduces required motor power and energy consumption without compromising lifting capability.

## 5. MAXIMIZE VALUE
This solution adds minimal components (counterweight, extended cables, guide rails) while delivering maximum benefit. It's passive, reliable, and has been proven in practice for over a century. No complex control systems or energy storage required.

---

## FINAL SOLUTION:

Install a heavy counterweight in the elevator shaft, connected to the car by cables running over pulleys at the top. The counterweight weighs approximately as much as the empty elevator car plus half of its typical passenger load. As the car rises, the counterweight descends on the opposite side; as the car descends, the counterweight rises.

This arrangement means the motor doesn't lift the full weight of the loaded car. Instead, it only needs to overcome the difference between the car's actual load and the counterweight. When the car is lightly loaded, the heavier counterweight helps pull it up. When fully loaded, the car is heavier and helps pull the counterweight up as it descends. The motor primarily provides the small additional force needed to overcome friction and any imbalance, rather than fighting gravity's full effect on the car's mass.

This system dramatically reduces the required motor size and energy consumption while maintaining full lifting capability for heavy loads across many floors.

**Why it works:** The counterweight converts the car's descent (normally wasted gravitational potential energy) into useful lifting force for the ascent, creating a balanced system where gravity assists rather than opposes the motor on every trip.

### anthropic/claude-sonnet-4.5 — `P_L_TRIZ_ON`
# TRIZ Analysis

## 1. IDENTIFY THE CONTRADICTION

**Technical contradiction:** 
- **Improving parameter:** Force/Power (we want to reduce the motor power needed to lift the car)
- **Worsening parameter:** Weight of moving object (the car plus passengers still has the same mass that must be raised against gravity)

This maps to the contradiction between reducing the force required while still moving the same weight through the same height.

## 2. SEARCH FOR RESOURCES

Available resources:
- **Gravity itself** – acts continuously and freely on all masses
- **The descending motion** – when the car goes down, gravity does work that is currently wasted
- **The weight of the car** – present whether going up or down
- **Empty space in the shaft** – room for additional elements
- **Existing structural support system** – cables, pulleys already needed

## 3. APPLY INVENTIVE PRINCIPLES

**Principle 8 - Counterweight (Anti-weight):** Compensate for the weight of an object by merging it with another object that provides lift, or use aerodynamic/hydrodynamic forces.

**Principle 6 - Universality:** Make one part perform multiple functions (the counterweight both balances the car and stores gravitational potential energy).

## 4. RESOLVE, DON'T TRADE OFF

Solution: Install a **counterweight system** consisting of a heavy mass (typically weighing about the same as the empty car plus 40-50% of its rated passenger capacity) connected to the elevator car via cables over pulleys. 

When the car ascends, the counterweight descends, and vice versa. The counterweight's falling weight pulls the car upward, while the car's falling weight pulls the counterweight upward. The motor only needs to overcome:
- The difference between car+load weight and counterweight
- Friction losses
- Acceleration forces

This directly resolves the contradiction: we still move the full weight, but the motor doesn't have to provide the full lifting force because the counterweight provides most of it through gravitational exchange.

## 5. MAXIMIZE IDEALITY

This solution has very high ideality:
- Uses gravity (free, always available resource)
- Adds only simple, passive components (weights, cables)
- No additional energy input required for the counterweight
- Works continuously without control systems
- The counterweight mass would exist anyway as structural load; we're just repositioning it to do useful work
- Reduces motor size, energy consumption, and operating costs dramatically
- Proven, reliable, simple to maintain

---

## FINAL SOLUTION:

Install a heavy counterweight connected to the elevator car by cables running over pulleys at the top of the shaft. The counterweight weighs approximately as much as the empty car plus half its maximum passenger load. As the car rises, the counterweight descends on the opposite side; as the car descends, the counterweight rises.

This arrangement means the two weights constantly balance each other through gravity. When lifting a half-loaded car, the motor works against almost zero net weight—it only overcomes friction and provides acceleration. Even when fully loaded, the motor lifts only the passengers' weight, not the car itself. When the car is empty and descending, the heavier counterweight actually assists the motor. The system recovers gravitational energy on every down trip and applies it to the next up trip.

**Why it works:** The counterweight transforms the problem from lifting dead weight against gravity into a balanced exchange where gravity pulls both directions simultaneously. The motor becomes a small control device managing the difference between two large, self-balancing forces rather than a massive engine fighting gravity alone.

### anthropic/claude-sonnet-4.5 — `P_S_TRIZ_OFF`
# Analysis and Solution Development

The core challenge is reducing the motor power and energy needed to lift a heavy elevator car repeatedly. The key insight is to balance the weight of the car so the motor only needs to overcome the difference in weight between the loaded and unloaded states, plus friction.

The solution is to use a **counterweight system**. Install a counterweight equal to approximately the weight of the empty car plus half the maximum passenger load. This counterweight hangs on the opposite side of the pulley system from the elevator car, connected by cables running over sheaves at the top of the shaft.

When the car ascends, the counterweight descends, and vice versa. The motor only needs to provide enough force to overcome:
1. The difference between the actual load and the counterweight
2. Friction in the system
3. Acceleration forces

This typically reduces the required motor power by 40-50% compared to lifting the full weight directly. The counterweight stores potential energy when descending, which assists the next upward journey.

---

# FINAL SOLUTION:

Install a heavy counterweight that hangs on cables on the opposite side of the elevator shaft from the car. The counterweight connects to the elevator car through steel cables that run over a large pulley (called a traction sheave) at the top of the shaft. Set the counterweight's mass to equal the empty elevator car plus about half of its maximum passenger capacity.

As the elevator car rises, the counterweight falls, and when the car descends, the counterweight rises. The motor mounted at the top only needs to make up the difference between whatever weight is actually in the car versus the fixed counterweight mass, plus overcome friction and provide acceleration. Instead of lifting the entire weight of a loaded car from the ground floor to the top, the motor just nudges the system in the right direction.

This arrangement dramatically reduces the motor size, power consumption, and operating costs. Most of the lifting work is done by gravity acting on the counterweight, not by the motor burning electricity.

**Why it works:** The counterweight provides a constant opposing force through gravity that nearly balances the elevator car's weight, so the motor expends energy only on the net difference rather than fighting the full weight on every trip.

### anthropic/claude-sonnet-4.5 — `P_S_TRIZ_ON`
# TRIZ Analysis

The contradiction here is that we need to lift a heavy load (required for function) but want to minimize the force/energy needed (required for efficiency).

**Relevant TRIZ Principles:**
- Principle 8: Counterweight - Balance the weight of an object with another object
- Principle 6: Universality - Make the object perform multiple functions to eliminate other parts

**Solution Approach:**
Use a counterweight system where a heavy weight on the opposite side of the pulley system balances most of the elevator car's weight.

# Solution

Install a counterweight system consisting of a heavy weight (typically concrete blocks or steel) attached to cables that run over pulleys in the elevator shaft. The counterweight hangs on the opposite side of the pulley from the elevator car. 

The counterweight is sized to equal the weight of the empty car plus approximately 40-50% of its maximum passenger load. When the car is at the bottom of the shaft, the counterweight is at the top, and vice versa.

As the car ascends, the counterweight descends, and the motor only needs to overcome the difference between the car's actual weight and the counterweight. When the car descends with a light load, the counterweight actually helps by pulling upward, and the motor acts as a brake.

This dramatically reduces the motor power needed—instead of lifting 2000+ pounds, the motor might only need to handle a 200-400 pound difference, reducing energy consumption by 40-50% and allowing a much smaller, less expensive motor and drive system.

**Why it works:** The counterweight provides a constant upward force that offsets most of the car's weight. The motor only supplies the incremental force needed to overcome the imbalance, transforming a large lifting problem into a small balancing problem.

---

FINAL SOLUTION:

Install a counterweight system in the elevator shaft. Attach a heavy weight—typically made of concrete blocks or steel plates—to steel cables that run over pulleys at the top of the shaft. This counterweight hangs on the opposite side of the pulley system from the elevator car itself.

The counterweight is carefully sized to equal the weight of the empty elevator car plus about forty to fifty percent of its maximum passenger capacity. As the elevator car moves up the shaft, the counterweight moves down, and when the car descends, the counterweight rises.

This arrangement means the motor doesn't have to lift the full weight of the loaded car. Instead, it only needs to overcome the difference between the car's current weight and the counterweight. When carrying a typical load, the system is nearly balanced, so the motor works minimally. When the car is heavily loaded going up, the motor adds force; when lightly loaded going down, it acts as a brake against the counterweight.

This reduces energy consumption by forty to fifty percent and allows use of a much smaller, less expensive motor than would otherwise be required.

### deepseek/deepseek-chat-v3.1 — `P_L_TRIZ_OFF`
**1. IDENTIFY THE CHALLENGE**  
The core trade-off is between **motor power/energy use** and **lifting capacity**. A powerful motor can lift the full dead weight but is large, expensive, and energy-intensive. Reducing motor size saves cost and energy but cannot lift the load. The tension is that the motor must be strong enough to lift the car yet small and efficient.

**2. SEARCH FOR RESOURCES**  
Available resources include: the counterweight mass already present in most elevators, the gravitational potential energy of the descending car or counterweight, and the empty space in the shaft. The descending load can be used to help lift the ascending load.

**3. APPLY DESIGN TACTICS**  
The relevant tactic is **counterweight balancing** combined with **energy regeneration**. Instead of treating the car’s weight as a dead load, balance it with a counterweight so the motor only supplies the difference. Then, capture the gravitational energy from the descending mass and reuse it.

**4. RESOLVE, DON'T TRADE OFF**  
By balancing most of the car’s weight with a counterweight, the motor sees only the net imbalance (e.g., the passenger load). This drastically reduces required motor power. Adding regenerative braking converts the descending energy into electricity, feeding it back to the building grid, cutting net energy use per trip.

**5. MAXIMIZE VALUE**  
This uses existing counterweight infrastructure and adds a regenerative drive, avoiding a massive motor. It delivers the lifting function with minimal added components—mainly an upgraded motor controller—while slashing energy costs.

**Why it works:**  
The counterweight offsets the constant car weight, so the motor handles only the variable passenger load. Regenerative braking captures energy during descent, recycling it instead of wasting it as heat. Together, they allow a smaller, cheaper motor and reduce net energy draw nearly to zero for balanced trips.

FINAL SOLUTION:  
Install a counterweight that weighs roughly the same as the empty elevator car, connected via cables over a sheave to the car. When the car goes up, the counterweight goes down, and their opposing weights mostly cancel out. The hoist motor then only needs to provide the extra force to lift the passengers inside, not the entire car. This allows the motor to be much smaller and less powerful. Furthermore, equip the motor with a regenerative drive system. When the heavier side (either the loaded car or the counterweight) descends, its gravitational pull turns the motor, which then acts as a generator. This generates electricity that can be fed back into the building’s electrical system, powering lights or other elevators. On a round trip with equal passenger load up and down, the net energy used can be very low, as the energy from the down trip partly powers the up trip. The system moves the car reliably while requiring a smaller motor and drastically cutting energy consumption and costs.

### deepseek/deepseek-chat-v3.1 — `P_L_TRIZ_ON`
1. **IDENTIFY THE CONTRADICTION.** The technical contradiction is between **energy use** (which we want to reduce) and **force** (which the motor must exert to lift the full weight). Improving energy efficiency worsens the required motor force and system size, or vice-versa. This can also be viewed as a physical contradiction: the counterweight system must be heavy to balance the load (reducing energy use) but light to avoid excessive inertia or cost.

2. **SEARCH FOR RESOURCES.** The system already contains the heavy elevator car and a vertical shaft. The primary available resources are gravity (a constant field) and the fact that the car’s downward trips are currently wasted—they could be used to store or recover energy. The empty space in the shaft and the mass of the car itself are also resources.

3. **APPLY INVENTIVE PRINCIPLES.** The most relevant principles are:
   - **Principle 1: Segmentation** – Divide the counterweight system into parts that can adapt.
   - **Principle 10: Preliminary Action** – Pre-set a counterbalancing mass.
   - **Principle 26: Copying** – Use a simple, inexpensive counterweight.
   - **Principle 35: Parameter Changes** – Change the weight of the counterweight dynamically.
   The core idea is **counterweight optimization**, which is a form of **Principle 24: Intermediary** (use an intermediary carrier) and **Principle 9: Preliminary Counteraction** (counteract the harmful force in advance).

4. **RESOLVE, DON'T TRADE OFF.** Instead of a fixed counterweight (which only balances the empty car), use a dynamic counterweight system that adjusts its effective mass to match the car’s load. This way, the motor only needs to supply the force for acceleration and friction, not the full weight difference, on every trip. This directly resolves the contradiction: energy use drops without requiring a larger motor.

5. **MAXIMIZE IDEALITY.** The ideal system uses the existing car’s motion to adjust the counterweight, adding minimal new components. A hydraulic or movable mass system within the counterweight frame, triggered by the load in the car, increases ideality by reusing the gravitational potential energy of the counterweight itself.

**FINAL SOLUTION:**

The elevator system is equipped with a counterweight that can change its effective mass to match the load inside the car. This is achieved by having a compartment within the counterweight structure that can be filled with or emptied of a dense, inexpensive material like sand or small metal shot. When passengers enter the car and select their floor, a load sensor measures the total weight. A signal is sent to the counterweight compartment, where a valve opens, allowing the correct amount of ballast material to flow in from a storage hopper at the top of the shaft, increasing the counterweight mass. If the car is lighter, the valve opens to release excess ballast into a collection bin at the bottom. The counterweight is now always nearly equal to the car's weight. The hoist motor only needs to overcome the small difference and friction, so it can be much smaller and more energy-efficient. During descent, the heavy car helps lift the counterweight, and the process reverses, recovering energy. This way, the system uses gravity and the mass of the ballast to do most of the work, drastically cutting energy use and motor size.

This works because it turns the counterweight from a static balance for an empty car into a dynamic partner that actively matches the load. The motor is freed from lifting dead weight, and the energy spent raising the ballast is mostly recovered when it is lowered, creating a near-balanced system that minimizes waste.

### deepseek/deepseek-chat-v3.1 — `P_S_TRIZ_OFF`
A practical solution is to use a counterweight that moves in the opposite direction to the elevator car. The counterweight is a heavy mass, typically weighing about the same as the empty car plus roughly half of its maximum passenger load. This counterweight is connected to the car by a cable that runs over a large, grooved pulley called a sheave at the top of the elevator shaft. When the motor turns the sheave to lift the car up, the counterweight descends. Conversely, when the car descends, the counterweight rises.

This system works because the counterweight balances a large portion of the car's weight. The motor no longer has to lift the entire dead load; it only needs to provide the force to lift the difference in weight between the loaded car and the counterweight, plus overcome friction. This dramatically reduces the power required from the motor, allowing for a smaller, more energy-efficient system. The descending counterweight also helps to slow the ascending car, reducing the energy needed for braking.

FINAL SOLUTION:

The solution is to balance the elevator car with a heavy counterweight that moves in the opposite direction. A counterweight, made of stacked metal weights, is suspended in the elevator shaft and connected to the top of the car by a strong steel cable. This cable loops over a large pulley driven by the motor. The counterweight is sized to weigh almost as much as the elevator car when it is about half full of people. When the elevator goes up to pick up passengers, the motor turns the pulley, pulling the car upward. At the same time, the counterweight, which is heavier than the empty car, descends and helps to pull the car up. This means the motor does very little work. When the car, now full and heavy, needs to go down, the situation reverses. The heavy car wants to descend, and it now helps to lift the lighter counterweight. Again, the motor only needs to control the speed and direction, using much less force. This balancing act means a much smaller, cheaper motor can be used, and it consumes far less electricity because it is primarily fighting friction and making small adjustments to the balanced system, not lifting the entire weight from scratch.

### deepseek/deepseek-chat-v3.1 — `P_S_TRIZ_ON`
A classic engineering challenge is to reduce the energy required to lift the elevator car. The core contradiction is between the need to lift a heavy load and the desire to minimize the power and energy consumption of the motor system. The Ideal Final Result would be for the elevator to ascend with minimal energy input, as if the car were weightless.

Several TRIZ principles are applicable. The principle of "Another Dimension" suggests using a vertical counterweight, which is the standard solution. However, this only balances the empty car weight, not the variable passenger load. The principle of "Pneumatics and Hydraulics" could involve using a hydraulic accumulator to store energy from the descending car, but this adds complexity. The most direct approach is to address the "Weight of Moving Object" parameter. The principle of "Preliminary Action" is key: we can pre-load a counterforce. A better application is the principle of "Counterweight," but enhanced. The principle of "Dynamicity" suggests making the counterweight system adaptive.

The solution is to use a counterweight that automatically adjusts its effective mass to match the car's load. This can be achieved by incorporating a water tank as part of the counterweight. When the car is loaded with passengers at the ground floor, water is pumped from a basement reservoir into the counterweight's tank, increasing its mass to balance the loaded car. When the car ascends, the motor only needs to overcome friction. Upon descent, the water is drained from the counterweight back to the reservoir, again balancing the system for the next trip. The energy for pumping is less than the energy needed to lift the full dead weight.

This works because it transforms the system from one that must constantly fight gravity to one that is nearly in balance. By dynamically matching the counterweight to the car's load, the motor's job is reduced to managing the small difference in weight and system friction, drastically cutting the required power and energy per trip.

FINAL SOLUTION:

The solution is to create an elevator system where a heavy car is balanced by an adjustable counterweight that changes its weight to match the load inside the car. This is done by building the counterweight as a large water tank. At the ground floor, before the elevator ascends, a pump transfers water from a storage tank in the building's basement up into the counterweight's tank. The amount of water pumped is calculated based on the weight of the passengers and cargo in the car. When the counterweight is filled with the correct amount of water, its downward pull nearly equals the upward pull of the loaded elevator car. This means the motor only has to provide a small amount of force to start the car moving and to overcome friction in the cables and guides, rather than lifting the entire mass. When the elevator needs to come back down, the water is drained from the counterweight tank back into the basement storage. This re-balances the system for the lightweight, empty car. The energy used to pump the water is much less than the energy that would be needed for a motor to directly lift the full weight of the passengers up many floors.

### google/gemini-2.5-flash — `P_L_TRIZ_OFF`
1.  **IDENTIFY THE CHALLENGE.**
    The core technical trade-off is between **motor power/energy consumption** and **lifting heavy loads**. To lift a heavy elevator car and its passengers, a powerful motor is required, leading to high energy consumption and a large, expensive drive system. The challenge is to reduce the required motor power and energy without reducing the load-carrying capacity. This is an internal design tension: the system must lift a heavy load (requiring high power) but also use low power.

2.  **SEARCH FOR RESOURCES.**
    *   **Gravity:** The weight of the elevator car and its passengers is a force acting downwards.
    *   **Empty space:** The elevator shaft itself.
    *   **Material properties:** The ability of materials to be heavy and dense.
    *   **By-products:** The downward motion of the elevator car when descending.

3.  **APPLY DESIGN TACTICS.**
    The most relevant tactic here is **Counterweighting** (a specific application of "Compensation" or "Prior Counter-action"). This tactic involves introducing an opposing force or mass to offset the primary force, thereby reducing the net force that the main system needs to overcome.

4.  **RESOLVE, DON'T TRADE OFF.**
    By adding a counterweight to the system, the motor no longer needs to lift the *entire* weight of the car and its load. Instead, it only needs to overcome the *difference* in weight between the car (plus its average load) and the counterweight, plus friction. This significantly reduces the required motor power and energy consumption. When the car descends, the counterweight rises, and the motor can even regenerate energy.

5.  **MAXIMIZE VALUE.**
    The counterweight is a simple, passive component that uses gravity (an existing resource) to directly offset the primary load. It adds minimal complexity and no active components, making it a highly efficient and cost-effective solution.

FINAL SOLUTION:
To efficiently move the heavy elevator car, a substantial counterweight is attached to the opposite end of the hoisting ropes. This counterweight is designed to weigh approximately the same as the empty elevator car plus about half of its maximum passenger capacity. As the elevator car ascends, the counterweight descends, and vice versa. The motor then only needs to provide enough power to overcome the difference in weight between the car (with its passengers) and the counterweight, rather than lifting the entire combined mass. This significantly reduces the workload on the motor, allowing for a smaller, less powerful, and more energy-efficient drive system. When the car descends, the heavier counterweight can even assist in pulling the car up, or the motor can act as a generator, returning energy to the building's electrical grid.

This works because the counterweight effectively balances most of the elevator's load, meaning the motor only has to manage the relatively small difference in weight between the car and its passengers and the fixed counterweight, rather than the full, heavy load.

### google/gemini-2.5-flash — `P_L_TRIZ_ON`
Here's a breakdown of the problem using the TRIZ methodology:

1.  **IDENTIFY THE CONTRADICTION.**
    *   **Improving Characteristic:** The ability to lift a heavy elevator car and its load (Force/Power).
    *   **Worsening Characteristic:** The size, cost, and energy consumption of the hoist motor (Weight/Energy Use).
    *   **Contradiction:** To lift a heavy load, the motor must be powerful (high Force/Power), but a powerful motor is large, expensive, and energy-intensive (high Weight/Energy Use). This is a classic trade-off between the useful function (lifting) and the harmful effect (resource consumption).

2.  **SEARCH FOR RESOURCES.**
    *   **Existing substances:** The elevator car itself, the passengers, the hoist ropes, the building structure.
    *   **Fields:** Gravity (acting on the car and passengers).
    *   **By-products/Waste:** The potential energy gained when the car is lifted, which is typically dissipated as heat when it descends.
    *   **Empty space:** The hoistway.
    *   **Properties of materials:** The strength of the ropes, the rigidity of the building.

3.  **APPLY INVENTIVE PRINCIPLES.**
    *   **Principle 1: Segmentation.** (Divide an object into independent parts). Not directly applicable to the core lifting problem.
    *   **Principle 10: Preliminary Action.** (Perform the required change completely or partially in advance). This could involve pre-tensioning or pre-balancing.
    *   **Principle 11: Prior Counter-action.** (Compensate for the relatively low efficiency of a field by combining it with another field).
    *   **Principle 14: Spheroidality – Curvature.** (Replace linear parts with curved ones, flat surfaces with spherical ones). Not directly applicable.
    *   **Principle 28: Mechanics Substitution.** (Replace a mechanical means with a sensory one; use electrical, magnetic, and electromagnetic fields to interact with the object).
    *   **Principle 29: Pneumatics and Hydraulics.** (Use gas and liquid parts of the system).
    *   **Principle 30: Flexible Shells and Thin Films.**
    *   **Principle 31: Porous Materials.**
    *   **Principle 32: Color Changes.**
    *   **Principle 35: Parameter Changes.** (Change the physical state of an object, its concentration or consistency, the degree of flexibility, the temperature).
    *   **Principle 36: Phase Transitions.**
    *   **Principle 37: Thermal Expansion.**
    *   **Principle 38: Strong Oxidants.**
    *   **Principle 39: Inert Atmosphere.**
    *   **Principle 40: Composite Materials.**

    The most relevant principles here are:
    *   **Principle 10: Preliminary Action** (specifically, pre-balancing the system).
    *   **Principle 11: Prior Counter-action** (using a counterweight to counteract the force of gravity).
    *   **Principle 28: Mechanics Substitution** (using a counterweight to reduce the mechanical work required from the motor).

4.  **RESOLVE, DON'T TRADE OFF.**
    By introducing a counterweight, we can effectively "pre-balance" the system. The counterweight, connected to the elevator car via the same hoist ropes and pulley system, acts as a constant opposing force to gravity. When the car goes up, the counterweight goes down, and vice-versa. The motor then only needs to overcome the *difference* in weight between the car (plus passengers) and the counterweight, plus friction, rather than the entire weight of the car and load. This significantly reduces the required motor power and energy consumption. The ideal counterweight is typically set to be equal to the weight of the empty car plus about half the maximum passenger load.

5.  **MAXIMIZE IDEALITY.**
    The counterweight system is highly ideal. It uses a simple, passive mass (a resource already present in the form of gravity and the need for a heavy object) to perform a significant portion of the work. It adds minimal complexity, no moving parts beyond its own movement, and no active energy input. It directly reduces the energy and power requirements of the main motor, making the overall system more efficient and less costly.

FINAL SOLUTION:
To efficiently move the heavy elevator car, a large counterweight is attached to the opposite end of the hoist ropes, running alongside the car in the elevator shaft. As the elevator car ascends, the counterweight descends, and vice-versa. This counterweight is typically designed to weigh roughly the same as the empty elevator car plus half of its maximum passenger capacity. The motor then only needs to provide enough power to overcome the difference in weight between the car (with its varying passenger load) and the counterweight, along with friction in the system. This significantly reduces the total force the motor must exert and the energy it consumes on each trip.

This solution works by leveraging the force of gravity itself. Instead of the motor lifting the entire weight of the car and passengers against gravity, the counterweight provides a constant opposing force. This effectively balances most of the load, allowing the motor to only handle the net imbalance and friction, drastically cutting down on the required power and energy for operation.

## Case 026
> A drug must be delivered into the body steadily over a long period. Taking it as small pills means dosing too often to be practical, while a single large dose meant to last the whole period would reach harmful concentrations.

### anthropic/claude-sonnet-4.5 — `P_L_TRIZ_OFF`
# STRUCTURED DESIGN PROCESS

## 1. IDENTIFY THE CHALLENGE
The core trade-off is between **dosing frequency** and **drug concentration safety**:
- Small frequent doses → safe concentration but impractically frequent administration
- Large single dose → convenient but dangerously high initial concentration

This is a conflict between the drug amount: it must be large (to last long) AND small (to stay safe).

## 2. SEARCH FOR RESOURCES
Available resources include:
- The body's own fluids and chemistry (stomach acid, enzymes, body temperature)
- Time itself as a resource
- Physical/chemical properties of materials (dissolution rates, permeability, degradation)
- Spatial distribution within a delivery system

## 3. APPLY DESIGN TACTICS
Primary tactic: **Segmentation in time** - divide the large dose into portions that release sequentially rather than all at once.

Supporting tactic: **Use of barriers/membranes** - control the rate at which drug becomes available through physical or chemical barriers.

## 4. RESOLVE, DON'T TRADE OFF
Create a multi-layer capsule where a large total drug amount is divided into concentric layers, each coated with a material that dissolves at different rates in the body. The outermost layer releases immediately, then successive inner layers release at timed intervals (e.g., 6, 12, 18 hours later) as their coatings dissolve. This provides the convenience of a single administration while maintaining safe concentrations throughout.

## 5. MAXIMIZE VALUE
This solution uses simple, proven coating materials (polymers that dissolve at predictable rates in digestive fluids) without requiring pumps, electronics, or surgical implantation. It's manufacturable with existing pharmaceutical technology.

---

FINAL SOLUTION:

The drug is packaged in a single capsule containing multiple small beads or pellets, each coated with a special material that dissolves at a different rate inside the body. When swallowed, the outermost coating on some pellets dissolves immediately, releasing the first dose. Other pellets have thicker coatings that take several hours to dissolve, releasing subsequent doses at predetermined intervals. Still others have even thicker coatings that dissolve after many more hours. By carefully selecting coating thicknesses and materials, the pellets can be designed to release drug steadily over days or even weeks from a single capsule.

The patient takes just one capsule, but receives the equivalent of many small doses spread over time. The total amount of drug is large enough to last the entire treatment period, yet at any given moment only a safe amount enters the bloodstream because most remains locked inside undissolved coatings.

This works because the coating materials act as timed barriers, transforming one large physical dose into many small chemical doses distributed across time, achieving both convenience and safety simultaneously.

### anthropic/claude-sonnet-4.5 — `P_L_TRIZ_ON`
# TRIZ Analysis

## 1. IDENTIFY THE CONTRADICTION

**Technical contradiction:** 
- Improving: Duration of action (one parameter: amount of substance delivered over time)
- Worsening: Harmful factors acting on object (concentration becomes toxic)

The system needs high total drug quantity (for long duration) but low instantaneous concentration (to avoid toxicity). This is also a **physical contradiction**: the drug quantity must be large (to last long) AND small (to stay safe).

## 2. SEARCH FOR RESOURCES

Available resources:
- The drug substance itself
- Time (the extended period is available as a resource)
- Body environment (temperature, moisture, pH, enzymes)
- Space within a delivery device
- Chemical/physical properties of materials (solubility, diffusion rates, degradation)

## 3. APPLY INVENTIVE PRINCIPLES

**Principle 1 - Segmentation:** Divide the drug into many small portions that release sequentially rather than all at once.

**Principle 10 - Prior Action:** Prepare the drug in a form that inherently releases slowly over time.

**Principle 28 - Mechanics Substitution:** Replace the mechanical action of repeated pill-taking with a physical/chemical field effect (diffusion, dissolution).

**Principle 35 - Parameter Change:** Change the physical state or properties of the drug carrier to control release rate.

## 4. RESOLVE, DON'T TRADE OFF

Combine Principles 1 (Segmentation) and 10 (Prior Action):

Create a **matrix-based controlled-release implant or patch** where the drug is embedded in a polymer matrix that erodes or allows diffusion at a controlled rate. The drug is segmented throughout the matrix in microscopic reservoirs. The polymer's degradation rate or porosity is engineered so drug molecules diffuse out steadily, maintaining therapeutic concentration without peaks.

Alternative implementation: **Osmotic pump capsule** - a single capsule with semi-permeable membrane. Body fluids enter through the membrane at a constant rate determined by osmotic pressure, pushing drug solution out through a laser-drilled hole at steady rate.

## 5. MAXIMIZE IDEALITY

The polymer matrix solution has high ideality:
- Uses existing resources (body moisture, temperature for diffusion/erosion)
- No external power needed
- Single administration replaces many doses
- Minimal complexity (just drug + polymer)
- The "system" (polymer) eventually disappears, leaving no waste

---

# FINAL SOLUTION:

Embed the drug uniformly throughout a biodegradable polymer matrix formed as a small implant placed under the skin or as a transdermal patch. The polymer is engineered with specific porosity and degradation characteristics that control how quickly body fluids penetrate and how fast drug molecules diffuse outward. As moisture from body tissues gradually permeates the matrix, drug particles dissolve and migrate through the polymer network at a steady, predictable rate determined by the material's physical structure. The polymer slowly breaks down into harmless byproducts that the body absorbs. By carefully selecting the polymer type, molecular weight, and crystallinity, manufacturers can tune the release duration from days to months. The drug dispersed throughout the matrix acts like thousands of tiny doses queued in three-dimensional space, each becoming available only as the diffusion front reaches it, maintaining constant therapeutic blood levels without dangerous peaks.

**Why it works:** The solution separates the total drug quantity in space and releases it progressively through time using passive diffusion, so the large total amount needed for long duration never appears as a dangerous concentration at any single moment.

### anthropic/claude-sonnet-4.5 — `P_S_TRIZ_OFF`
# Analysis and Solution Development

The core challenge is controlling the release rate of medication to maintain therapeutic levels without reaching toxic concentrations or requiring frequent dosing.

**Solution Approach:**

Create an implantable or ingestible device using a semi-permeable membrane system. The drug is contained within a reservoir surrounded by a specially engineered polymer membrane that allows controlled diffusion. The membrane's pore size, thickness, and material properties determine the release rate.

**Concrete Implementation:**

A small cylindrical capsule (5-15mm) contains the drug in concentrated form. The outer layer consists of a rate-controlling ethylcellulose or similar polymer membrane. Water from body fluids penetrates the membrane, gradually dissolving the drug core, which then diffuses outward at a constant rate determined by the membrane properties. The device can be implanted subcutaneously or, for shorter durations, swallowed as an extended-release capsule.

For longer-term delivery (weeks to months), a subcutaneous implant about the size of a rice grain can be inserted with a simple procedure and removed when depleted.

**Why It Works:**

The semi-permeable membrane acts as a physical barrier that limits how quickly the drug can exit the reservoir, regardless of the concentration inside. The diffusion rate through the membrane remains relatively constant because it depends on the membrane's fixed properties rather than the drug concentration gradient alone. This creates a zero-order release kinetic, delivering steady amounts over extended periods.

---

FINAL SOLUTION:

The solution is a controlled-release capsule or small implant that uses a special outer coating to regulate how quickly medication enters the body. The drug sits inside a tiny reservoir surrounded by a semi-permeable polymer membrane—a thin barrier with microscopic pores. This membrane allows body fluids to slowly seep in and dissolve the concentrated drug inside, while simultaneously letting the dissolved medication diffuse outward at a carefully controlled rate.

For short-term use (days to weeks), patients can swallow an extended-release capsule. For longer periods (months), a doctor can insert a small implant under the skin—about the size of a grain of rice—using a quick, minimally invasive procedure. The membrane's thickness, pore size, and chemical composition are precisely engineered during manufacturing to achieve the desired release rate for each specific medication.

The membrane acts as a physical bottleneck that limits drug flow regardless of how much medication remains inside. Water gradually enters, dissolves some drug, and that dissolved drug slowly exits through the same membrane at a steady pace, maintaining safe and effective blood levels throughout the treatment period without requiring repeated doses.

### anthropic/claude-sonnet-4.5 — `P_S_TRIZ_ON`
# TRIZ Analysis

The contradiction here is between the duration of drug delivery (needs to be long) and the concentration safety (must remain within therapeutic range). This is a typical TRIZ contradiction between "Duration of action of a moving object" and "Harmful factors."

Relevant TRIZ principles include:
- **Principle 10 (Preliminary Action)**: Prepare the drug in a form that releases gradually
- **Principle 24 (Intermediary)**: Use an intermediate mechanism to control release
- **Principle 28 (Mechanics Substitution)**: Replace mechanical systems with chemical/physical phenomena

# Solution Approach

Create a subcutaneous implant using a biodegradable polymer matrix embedded with drug particles. The polymer slowly erodes through hydrolysis, releasing the drug at a controlled rate over weeks or months. The implant is a small rod (about matchstick-sized) inserted under the skin through a minor procedure.

The polymer degradation rate determines release kinetics. By selecting appropriate polymer composition (e.g., PLGA - poly(lactic-co-glycolic acid) with specific lactide:glycolide ratios), the release profile can be precisely tuned to maintain therapeutic drug levels throughout the treatment period.

---

FINAL SOLUTION:

Insert a small rod-shaped implant about the size of a matchstick under the skin through a quick minor procedure. This implant consists of the medication embedded throughout a special biodegradable polymer material. Once placed under the skin, body fluids gradually break down the polymer through natural chemical processes. As the polymer slowly dissolves over time, it steadily releases small amounts of the medication into the surrounding tissue, where it enters the bloodstream.

The key is choosing the right polymer material that breaks down at exactly the rate needed for that particular drug. Different polymer compositions dissolve at different speeds—some over weeks, others over months or even years. By matching the polymer breakdown rate to the desired treatment duration, the implant delivers a constant, safe dose throughout the entire period. When the polymer fully dissolves, both it and the medication are safely absorbed by the body, leaving nothing behind to remove.
